The Co-Operative
SYSTEM, the nearest approach to Socialism that can be achieved under the
present conditions of life, has met with phenomenal success wherever it has
been applied to any object. In England, even the Co-operative Societies
benefit some millions of persons—their membership exceeding a million and
a-half, and hold invested capital to the amount of eleven millions of money.
This success is owing to the fact that the investor sees not only a fair com-
mercial return on his investment, but a proprietary division of profits, which
he would not receive from any other organisation. It has been left to Aus-
tralia to apply this communistic principle to
Irrigation
and the CO-OPERATIVE IRRIGATION and MERCANTILE SOCIETY
of AUSTRALIA, Ltd., have already secured three irrigation colonies in
Victoria, which are partially settled and planted, and are negotiating for the
purchase of a tract of magnificent irrigation country in the West. Nego-
tiations are also pending for a Government concession of Murray land, and
there are now
Colonies
half-populated, awaiting the small Capitalist, the employed ARTISAN, or
the man whose sole CAPITAL is his LABOUR.

SATURDAY, AUGUST 15, 1891.
A Governor on Grab.
“ The dear old mother country ” is a phrase
continually mouthed by Australian Imperial-
ists. Whenever they get a good chance they
remind anyone who may be listening that
Australians owe everything to that affec-
tionate and self-sacrificing Alma Mater. As
a general thing they choose banquets and
layings of foundation-stones, and other
“ functions ” of that kind, for expression of
their sentiments. On such occasions they
are able, as it were, to get an audience down
and hit it, without their being subjected to
the risk of being struck back. Proposing a
toast affords them a famous chance. They
will drag in “The dear old,” &c,, when
giving “ The Queen, goblesser.” But they
brim over with delight w hen they can hitch
their remarks on to “ The land we live in.”
Then it is that they really revel in imported
patriotism. They are proud to claim the
name of Britons. (Cheers.) All Australians
are Britons, and it is highly creditable to
them. (Cheers.) All Britons are superior
to everybody else—(cheers) —but the genuine
stock is centred in the dear old mother
country. (Emotion.) There exists the stud-
farm, so to speak. The gentry who thus
hold forth know perfectly well that Young
Australia chafes at such remarks, but is too
courteous to hoot on a social occasion, and
hasn’t a chance of replying. Moreover,
indeed, when they hook their remarks on to
“The Queen,” anyone who should look un-
pleasant would be charged with insulting
a lady—unmanly wretch ! It is people
of this Jingo quality, who are in the
habit of affirming that the dear old
mother country displayed unparalleled
generosity when she conferred on the several
Australian colonies the right to freely deal
with the lands within their respective boun-
daries. It is these same gentry who assert
that the dear old mother country has shown
extraordinary benevolence in lending these
colonies two hundred millions sterling or so
on public account and about half as much
again as private advances.
* # #
That the territory generously granted to
the people who occupied and explored it
was supposed to be worthless or thereabouts
at the time the concession was made disturbs
those Jingoes not at all. To hear them,
it might be thought that the genuine, pure-
bred Britons who stay at home had, by their
extraordinary talents and exertions,” made
Australia habitable and valuable, and there-
upon, with gorgeous self-denial, bestowed it
upon the thankless and undeserving persons
who happened to be loafing in the territory.
One would also imagine that the lent three
hundred millions or so, had been presented
unconditionally in the purest spirit of philan-
thropy and of natural affection, without any
stipulation for repayment or for so much
interest per shent. The common, vulgar
Australian, however, recognises these truths
and listens with undemonstrative contempt
to the mawkish blatherings of after-dinner
Imperialists. He has arrived at the know-
ledge that the dear old mother country has
never made one single sacrifice for Aus-
tralia. He is j)et’fectly acquainted with
the fact that in the seventies, at a
time when the British Island Britons
were of opinion that these colonies cost
more than the profit derived from them,
a deliberate and pretty strong hint was
thrown out to Australians that there would
be no objection offered Avere they to dis-
solve partnership with the old country. He
is aware, moreover, that the parental affec-
tion, since so much vaunted, never carried
away the people of Great Britain to any
outbursts of generosity, in any respect dif-
ferent from ordinary speculative dealings
Avith alien communities; and that money
Avas just as readily provided to satisfy the
applications of Chilians and Argentines
and Uruguayans and Turks and Egyptians
and Russians and Portuguese as it AA'as
to “ ad\ 7 ance the interests of our brethren
in the Antipodes.” The events consequent
on the national aspirations in Egypt, of
which the reA r olt headed by Arabi was the
manifestation, have also opened the eyes of
the common Australian to Avhat might be
the result Avere any indication given that
Australia might be disposed to snap “the
crimson thread of kinship ” and the gilt-
steel bond of dependence Avhich c institute
the British money-lenders’ security for their
investments in these colonies.
* * *
If Australians were not fully alive to these
aspects of the Imperial connection the
recently - published despatch by Lord
Kintore, Governor of South Australia, to
the Secretary of State for the Colonies,
Avould have sufficed to arouse him. Lord
Kintore is one of the batch of gold-plated
persons of title, which was recently dis-
patched by the Tory Government of Great
Britain to officiate in the Australian colonies,
in response to the declarations of “returned
Australians,” travelling Companions of
Saints Michael and George, “leading”
journalists, and other snobs avlio pre-
tended to be, and Avere accepted as being,
representatives of Australian public feeling.
Lately, Lord Kintore took a trip across the
continent from South to North, in the
course of which he traversed what is termed
the Northern Territory, a tract placed pro-
visionally under the administration of the
South Australian Government. On the
strength of his couple of years’ residence in
Australia and this bit of OA r erlanding, the
Peer, who is unconscious that, despite pro-
longed habitation and this trip, he is still
ranked among newchums, assumes airs, as is
not uncommon Avith newchums at that stage,
of authority on the most difficult problems
of Australian settlement. It Avas at precisely
the same stage of Australian experience that
Julian Thomas, known in literary circles as
“ The Vagabond,” visited North Queensland,
as a special commissioner for the Melbourne
Argus, at a time when the statesmen and
old inhabitants of the Northern cohmy were
Avrestling with the problem how to deal with
the Chinese question. Mr. Thomas, after
a short residence in Cooktown, convulsed
all Queensland Avith laughter by Aviring to
his employers that he had arrived at a settle-
ment of the Avhole difficulty. Now, Mr.
Thomas is, by virtue of native endowments,
an immeasurably abler man than Lord Kin-
tore, and he brought to the investigation he
Avas charged Avith, qualifications such as few
men possessed and to which Lord Kintore
is entirely a stranger. Mr. Thomas had led
a roving life, had visited many regions of the
world, and had sharpened his powers of
observation and judgment by attrition on all
sorts, conditions, and races of men. Lord
Kintore, on the other hand, is simply an
Eton and Oxford young man ; a Park and
Piccadilly young man. Yet, he has ventured
to assert his competency to settle, after just
the same inadequate Australian experience,
the question, an alleged settlement of which
under like conditions, overwhelmed Mr.
Thomas beneath the ridicule of experienced
colonists.
* * *
Even this exploit has not satisfied Lord
Kintore’s self-complacency. Besides settling
the Chinese question, he disposes of the
future of all tropical Australia, and sum-
marily adjudges the much-vexed questions of
a transcontinental raihvay and wholesale land
alienation. Into a single dispatch to Loi’d
Knutsford, this eminently rash young man
crowds a farrago of erroneous “facts,” pre-
posterous inferences, and mischievous anti-
Australian suggestions. His Lordship pro-
nounces that the average Avhite man is
incapable of sustained exertion in tropical
Australia, and that European Avomen and
children do not thrive in these regions. Both
of these assertions are capable of disproof.
There are thousands of men and hundreds of
Avomen who can controvert them from their
own experience. The 'assertion has, in
fact, been made and disproved over
and over again, long before Lord
Kintore ever thought of Australia.
And it has always been advanced by the
same class of persons, and Avith the same
ulterior purpose. That purpose is ordin-
arily kept somewhat in the background, but
Lord Kintore, being a more than usually
green disputant, trots it out in his very next
sentence. “It is clear,” he remarks, Avith
innocent candour, “ that no employer Avould
be likely to continue to pay 10s. a day to
Europeans for labour which, as a rule, they
are incapable of satisfactorily performing,
seeing that Asiatics can work continuously
and satisfactorily at a much lower wage."
And he is, unfortunately, enabled to support
his position by reference to the fact that
the South Australian Legislature has quite
recently passed an Indian Coolie Immigra-
tion Act for application to the Northern
Territory. His Lordship is even less happy in
the next bit of testimony he adduces. This
is the unhappy condition of agriculture in
North Queensland —an unhappy condition
which he gives Lord Knutsford to under-
stand is due to the failure of white men to
endure labour in the climate. It so happens,
on the contrary, that the stagnation of
agriculture in North Queensland may, with
more justice, be attributed to the failure of
the very course which the Governor of S A.
recommends the introduction of cheap
coloured or Asiatic labour. Not that slave-
labour might not be made to pay, but because
the white colonists of Queensland simply will
not permit it to exist. Lord Kintore was
absolutely ignorant at the time when he
penned his dispatch that the owners of
plantations in tropical Queensland had
found a solution for the difficulties into
which they had been plunged by the prohi-
bition of coloured labour, and that the
solution was simply to sell their plantations
in moderate-sized lots to white settlers on
the instalment principle, with assistance in
the way of implements, while undertaking
to buy and manufacture the raw products of
these white farmers’ industry.
* * *
The absolute ignorance of Lord Kintore
on this point is no inference of ours. It is
clearly expressed by words in the dispatch.
“ Asyour Lordship is aware,” he writes, “the
Queensland Act for Indian Coolie Labour has
been repealed, the Polynesian Labour Act
expires next year, and the Chinese are
rigorously excluded. It is therefore difficult
to see what course the Queensland sugar-
planters can adopt to prevent their
machinery standing idle, or, indeed, to avoid
the abandonment of their plantations.”
Prompted by the imagined plenitude of
information and experience derived from
a canter from Adelaide to Port Darwin, and
by his actual wealth of ignorance, this
glittering new-chum proceeds to trifle with
the destinies of all Australia, He impeaches
the judgment of Australian legislators in
Brisbane and Adelaide as beingthatof persons
“ mainly ignorant of the conditions of tropi-
cal life, and swayed by constituencies
having little interest in or prejudiced against
the methods and management of tropical
colonies.” This is perhaps the most magnifi-
cent display of supercilious and self-satisfied
newchumishness in the whole of the remark-
able dispatch we are considering. Poor
Lord Kintore has evidentally fallen pros-
trate into the mental self-sufficiency which
more or less bewrays all immigrants at his
stage of colonial experience. He knows
everything, and all older colonists lack ex-
perience and sense. No one has ever done
any overlanding such as he has accomplished,
and inasmuch as he has visited the tropics,
all other inhabitants of Australia have
omitted to do so. It follows, of course, that
he considers himself competent to teach
Australians how to manage their own
business. This frame of mind is so common
among new-chums that it would merely
evoke the customary smile, were it not that
this particular greenhorn is Governor of a
colony and is thus in a position to embody
his fatuousness in the form of a dispatch,
and present it as serious advice to the
Government of Great Britain.
* * *
Regarded in this light, Lord Kintore’s
conclusions are past a joke, and may be
productive of the gravest consequences.
On the grounds stated, he has under-
taken to influence the Government of South
Australia to the most unpatriotic expedients,
and the Government of Great Britain to
adopt a coui'se which would inflict desperate
damage upon the Australian Commonwealth.
“I am in hopes,” he writes, “that my
Government may see their way to undertake
to do their utmost to render operative legis-
lation providing for the supply of Indian
Coolie Labour for a stated term of 9 years,
in order to attract to the Northern Territory
enterprise which has been killed in Queens”-
land, and capital to carry it on.” Not
content with this, the South Australian
Governor proceeds to hint that it might be
just as well were the Northern Terri-
tory taken out of the hands of Aus-
tralians altogether and converted into
a Crown Colony. Such a suggestion,
if not rebutted by the most determined
remonstrances on the part of the constitu-
tionally-governed colonies, is but too apt to
commend itself to the grabbing financiers
and syndicate-mongers of Great Britain.
Since Western Australia received a consti-
tution there has been no part of this con-
tinent in which a British Minister could order
a free gift of a million acres to be granted to
a globe-trotting nobleman, as Avas done there
in the case of the late guinea-pig Duke of
Manchester. Were the Northern Territory
withdrawn from Australian control this plea-
sant process could be revived. In accord-
ance with the spirit and purport of Lord
Kintore’s recommendation, huge tracts
might be granted to British land-grabbers on
the agreeable condition that they undertook
to import so many Chinese or Indian coolies
to work on the land. By supplementing this
with the adoption of the land-grant system
of trans-continental railway construction -
ikevvise to be accompanied by the introduc-
tion of Oriental labour —the entire social and
political fabric of Australian existence could
be undermined. The conditions which
brought about the civil war in North America
could be thus implanted in Australia. And
all these miseries because the busybodies in
London, called returned Australians, and the
wealthy lower orders who constitute the
Government House sets in our own capitals,
have managed to persuade the Colonial Office
that our essentially democratic communi-
ties “dearly love a lord” to preside over
their affairs, and don’t mind how verdant or
how gawky the lord may be.
* * *
It is requisite that a peremptory stop
should be put to Lord Kintore’s impertin-
ences and to all similar interferences with
the destinies of Australia by young gentle-
men exported to satisfy the unwholesome
cravings of the snob-circles after persons oi
title to rub against. The publication ot
Lord Kintore’s most mischievous dispatch
can only be attributed to a most exceptional
accident. The complacency of his Lordship
has been so supreme that he has been per-
fectly satisfied that his ravings are exemplars
of profoundest statesmanship, and certain
to be acceptable to every sensible person in
Australia It is impossible to know how
many more dispatches of like tendency have
emanated from British Governors in the
several colonies. But it is natural, after
having been let behind the scenes in this
instance, that uneasy conjectures should assail
Australians, that this sort of thing may have
been going on for a long time in the confiden-
tial communications between Governors and
Secretaries of State. It appears that while
our Legislatures have been playing at Fede-
ration conferences and all the rest of it,
there has appeared to the mind of at least
one British Governor of an Australian pro-
vince no incompatibility in supposing that
the Colonial Office would calmly accept and
act upon suggestions that a big slice of the
continent should be withdrawn from Aus-
tralian control and converted into a receiving
depot for Chinamen and Hindoos, who would
unfailingly filter past the borders of the
slave-state so created, and, by invading the
constitutionally -governed provinces, over-
turn and destroy all those social and
industrial arrangements which the European
population are working out for themselves.
To Lord Kintore, the proposals embodied
in his dispatch bear, no doubt, an aspect of
judicious, although somewhat superior and
uncommon, sense. He will probably be
surprised to learn that, in the opinion of The
Bulletin, they are pregnant with the seeds
of insurrection, bloodshed, and separation or
subjection.
Wanted: A Code.
It is a healthy sign to see a lawyer leading the
attack on the venerable iniquity miscalled Law.
The stronghold of chartered injustice has held
out stubbornly for many centuries against
the assaults of reason and progress and en-
lightenment ; but when its own defenders begin
to come out, we may hope that the walls are
growing shaky. The Labour-party in the New
(South Wales Assembly will do well to keep
their eyes on George Reid, who may be very
useful to them by and by. Their present and
only business is to get Electoral Reform. “ One-
man-one-vote ” is the most important plank in
their platform yet. There will be no candid,
straightforward, unforced legislation in the in-
terests of the people until the legislature
truly represents the people. Bur when that
does come about, then anybody who has any
sensible practical scheme for bringing the
system of law and legal procedure into
something like harmony with popular ideas will
be welcome to step forward and unpack his
soul. It is the imperative duty of a genuinely
democratic legislature to see that the laws of
the country reflect the will of the people of the
country, and there will have to be a great cart-
ing away of mouldy legal rubbish before that
can be said, of the laws of any of the Australias,
or indeed of any square foot of British territory
under the sun. Perhaps George Reid can
assist in sorting out from the rubbish the little
useful material that, having hitherto been over-
looked by the lawyers, has inadvertently been
allowed to remain.
* * *
The only system of law-reform that can be
expected to give satisfaction to the people of
Australia is a system that goes down to first
principles and makes a fresh start. We need
not trouble ourselves about the jar that it will
give the “Constitution” and the venerable
superstitions of our ancestors. Our ancestors
are comfortably packed away underground,
thank Heaven, and when the jumble of cranky
atrocities that those old humorists of the
middle ages jocularly called a legal system has
been duly interred along with them, it will
make the air clearer for to-day’s generation.
We may not be any wiser than our forefathers ;
but a good many things that were good enough'
for them are not good enough for us.
* # #
Our ancestors made laws as they wanted
them to suit their own circumstances, or rather
the laws were made for our ancestors by, and
to suit those who were bossing them at the
time. We have the right to make our own
laws, and they should be made to suit our cir-
cumstances. We are more or less free Aus-
tralians, living close up against the far end of
the nineteenth century, and the chances are not
in favour of our being properly suited with laws
or anything else that were intended for English
serfs when the centuries were just beginning to
get into their teens. We have no call to deal
tenderly with old laws. There is no sanctity
about them. The English nation didn’t get
their code handed down from Sinai on tables of
stone. Laws have no more claim to reverence


of partnership or the articles of
tba, N«tion of a joint-stock company. In fact,
ass ociatio whj J. the laws 0 f a nation amount
t iat Hitherto the British race has allowed the
H Lc run in the interests of the
concern _ now t i ie shareholders have come
to’have a’sav in the matter, it is time to over-
Sul the articles of association and re-cast them
from end to end.
# * *
The simple question is, whether two or three
ill inn Australians, freeborn in theory, and
*v u .* „ nrettv vigorous determination to
Sl °kfi 1 themselves free in fact, are or are not fit
Sty down for themselves the rules by which
their transactions with one another are to be
governed. If they arc, then why should they
Slow themselves to be overborne in all their
civU relations by a horde of king-cursed and
1: ridden semi-barbarians who formerly
Sffed the distant British Islands? Why
should they suffer their criminal-law policy to
Lp dictated by the unmitigated barbarians who,
in the inscrutable wisdom of Providence were
iiprmitted to invent and put in practice a
B er , lirv 'iso the “ system ” that turned the
hemisphere into a seething hell ? Will
’ member of Parliament, after reading, say,
the New South Wales Criminal Law Amend-
ment Act steadily through, venture to say in
„old blood that he considers it to be
such a code of criminal jurisprudence as
a young, free, and enlightened nation
m i 2 hb adopt with a sense of satisfaction,
if it were turning its hands to the law-making
business for the first time, and had no traditions
or rooted prejudices to take account of i If
not why should it remain on the statute-book ?
If it suits the people, if it be adapted to their cir-
cumstances, by all means let it stay. Though it
is a gory anachronism, a relic of the times thar
we have put behind us, a companion horror to
the triangles and the cat, it may be that a
majority of the people’s representatives have
no fault to find with it—that they are quite
satisfied to live in a country where a man, who
wants to be hanged, can take his pick out of
one of the choicest collections of capital offences
to be found anywhere on the surface of the
earth. Then give them an opportunity of
saying so. Let them take on themselves the
responsibility of re-enacting the statute, section
by section.
* * *
The one merit of the criminal law in New
South Wales is that there is very little doubt
about it. If you want to know the charge for
any given diversion—from picking your neigh-
bour’s pocket to cutting his throat —you have
only to refer to Sir Alfred Stephen’s carefully-
compiled and comprehensive tariff. There is
no such royal road to learning on the civil side
of law in any of the Australasias. The only
way to ascertain for certain what your
rights and liabilities are, under a contract which
you may have been foolish enough to sign,
is to bring an action on it in the Supreme
Court, and then appeal to the Privy Council.
If you succeed you will find that you are
entitled to pay a large sum for costs, and if you
are lucky subsequently the Insolvency Court
authorities will not suspend your certificate for
more than three months. But the point of law
will be settled. Nobody knows what the law
is on most points in any Australian province.
That is the sole reason why it continues to be
the law. The first duty of the law-reformer is
to get a man, or half-a-dozen men, appointed to
write out in plain English a concise statement
of what the law under each of its branches is
supposed to be. That would do the whole
business. No argument would be needed.
Somebody would at once bring in a Bill of one
clause to this effect; —‘ ‘ Be it enacted that all
laws in force are repealed from this date.”
And it would pass through Parliament in
one sitting. Then there would be a clean sheet
for the commencement of a code that would
not look like the production of a medieval
crusader.
# # #
There was a crafty statute passed in the reign
of his sacred Majesty Charles 11., a little over
200 years ago, “ for the prevention of frauds
and perjuries.” That Act and the osculation
of the Holy Scripture are still the chief safe-
guards in Australia as well as English Courts
of alleged Justice against false oaths. If a
plaintiff, suing a defendant for the price of a
suit of clothes, goes into the box and swears
that he made the suit for a third person on the
defendant verbally “guaranteeing” payment, he
will be promptly nonsuited because the law con-
siders that no man can be trusted to swear the
truth in a matter of that sort. But if the
plaintiff has been there before, he swears
that the defendant said “ You make the clothes,
and I’ll pay you,” and gains a verdict. It’s the
same individual, and he kisses the same greasy
Bible; but while, if he swears to one form of
words, his evidence is admissible, he is not
allowed to swear to the other for fear he should
perjure himself. In the same way the man
who sues his employer on an unwritten engage-
ment to take him on for 12 months certain,
finds that his case turns entirely on the question
whether he is prepared to swear that the 12
months was to commence the day the engage-
ment was made, or the next day. If he has
had anybody to put him up to the statute “ for
the prevention of frauds and perjuries,” he
kisses the book cheerfully and swims ahead ; it
not, he comes a cropper. There were no doubt
reasons for enacting a law of that kind which
were perfectly satisfactory to its hydrocephalous
authors in the year of grace 1677. We don’t
complain of their passing it. But why should
such a piece of legislation, with its brother
abortions on every page of the statute-book,
hang round our necks in the last decade of the
nineteenth century ? We must do justice,
however, to our forefathers. There is Aus-
tralian legislation dating within the decade
that antiquity would find it hard to
beat. It may not be generally known
that by the Criminal Law Amendment Act of
■New South Wales every married woman in the
province —unless entitled to a decree annulling
her marriage—could lay an information against
her husband rendering him liable to fourteen
years’ penal servitude and three floggings. The
knowledge of this fact should be balm to some
of the more strident spinsters of the Women’s
Suffrage League. It meets one of their choicest
grievances.
* # *
And once more The Bulletin says : Wipe it
all clean out, and let ns have a code—not a
mere compilation from the hands of some Cock-
ney lawyer-knight, but an adaptation to the
needs of Australia of the best laws of the most
democratic countries of the world.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Two Governors-Creaeral.
The news that the British Tory Government
propose to establish a censorship of the native
press in India, and the cablegram that
some native papers are to be prosecuted for
sedition, is an instructive commentary on
a good many different subjects. The Indian
press is the mouthpiece of some 270,000,000
souls, forming more than three-fourths of the
population of the entire British Empire. It is
the one and only means by which this vast
mass of humanity can protest against the
exactions of British officials and the lop-sided
justice of British magistrates—against the
ruinous policy of extravagance which has loaded
India with an enormous debt and a vast mass
of taxation—against the lying of British diplo-
macy, the forcible introduction of landlordism
in place of the old system of common tenure
which once prevailed in many parts of the
country, and a hundred other grievances. There
are no Parliamentary institutions in the coun-
try—there is no modern history, except that
which is written by British historians for the
purpose of demonstrating the benefits and
advantages of the Christian autocracy —there is
absolutely no means of protest except through
the press, and even it has never ventured to
protest save in a minor key, for fear of the
irresponsible satrap at Calcutta. Yet even the
feeble remonstrance of Indian journalism is to
be smothered for the future, and three-fourths
of Britain’s subjects will thus lose the last
semblance of human liberty. There is a moral
in this which is particularly appropriate in
these days when Federation is “in the air.”
What the Governor-General of India does is
exactly what the Governor-General of United
Australia would do if the opportunity pre-
sented itself, and for that reason Australia
must take no chances and run no risks. The
Indian Viceroy has the power to run an autocratic
Government on the Russian model—to pile up
an immense debt and weigh the nation down
with the burden of a huge army—to fill the
offices of state with a countless swarm of
corrupt, expensive, and useless officials—to
arrest and imprison any native of influence who
is not sufficiently subservient to the Foreign
invaders, and to gag the press —and, simply
because he has the power, he does all these
things. The old, benevolent drivel about a
civilising mission, and an inborn love of free-
dom, and such matters count for nothing ; the
requisite brute force is available, and therefore
Britain’s representative goes the whole hog
without hesitation. And in like manner the
Commonwealth Bill gives the coming Australian
viceroy authority to create a Ministry out of his
own friends and hangers-on—to keep that
Ministry in power either with or without the
consent of Parliament—and to do as he darned
well likes without consulting the Executive
Council if it pleases him, and, because he has
the power, the imported Governor-General of
Australia will do these things, provided a crisis
should arise which will make it worth his while
to run the risk. The two Governors-General
will be built of the same material, will be men
of the same class and the same nation, with
the same prejudices'and the same training,
and will receive their instructions from
the same Government ; and both starting
from the same causes they will arrive at the
same results. When the Parkes Government
issued its mysterious pamphlet explaining away
the anomalies and discrepancies of the great
Convention bungle, it was claimed in that
historic document that all these extraordinary
concessions to Imperialism mattered nothing,
because the Governors-General would never take
advantage of the powers conferred upon them.
It is sufficient answer to this assumption that
the other Governor-General—he of Calcutta —is
endowed with still greater powers, and that he
uses them to the uttermost whenever the
interests of British supremacy demand it. And
as his brother and duplicate in Australia will
be sent out to represent British interests in
exactly the same way, he will act in just the
same manner when the occasion requires it, and
the occasion will require it the very first day
Australia refuses to be sacrificed to British
policy. If the Convention Bill passes in its
present suicidal form, then when England’s
next big war comes along, and this country fails
to see why Sydney and Melbourne should be
bombarded or burnt in England’s quarrel, it
will l)o discovered that the two Governors-
General are wonderfully alike after all.

N aturalisation.
Ninian Melville, M.P. (N.S.W.), wants to
make the naturalisation of aliens more difficult
of obtainal, by grafting on to the preliminary
five years’ residence and the twenty-shillings’
qualification - fee an examination in English
literature and law. The qualification for voting
now existing is purely a residential one, and it
would not only be ridiculous, but unjust, to
substitute for it, in racial cases only, an intel-
lectual standard. Many Germans, on arrival,
know more, from a literary point of view, of
British literature and law than a great
proportion of the residents of Australia.
Is it right that those men should be dis-
qualified from voting on account of a presum-
able intellectual deficiency, or a lack of oral
volubility in the use of English ? Surely not.
The lack of acquaintance with English forms of
speech does not absolve the foreigner from
subjection to Australian laws, whether framed
here or borrowed from Britain; it does not
release him from his obligations to contribute
to the revenue. As a matter of fact, the resid-
ence of an alien in Australia imposes on him
all the obligations, moral and monetary, of
citizenship, while depriving him, for a time, of
its highest privilege —the right, by selection of a
representative, to have a voice in the making
and administration of the laws in this land of
his adoption. The Bulletin, unlike Mr. Mel-
ville, M.L.A., is cosmopolitan, and would like
to sec naturalisation made easier of accomplish-
ment. In America the alien is admitted in
most States after six months’ residence (four
months’ in Minnesota) to all the privileges of
American citizenship, on making statutory de-
claration of desire to enjoy that citizenship,
coupled with a casting aside of allegiance pre-
viously given to outside princes, potentates,
and powers. Some day the intelligent Britisher
arriving in Australia will require to abjure
himself, by statutory declaration, of his
oath of allegiance to the House of Guelph ;
meantime, the law which makes five years’
residence and a twenty-shilling fee the con-
ditions of Australian citizenship to the intelli-
gent foreigner, is undemocratic, stupid and
tyrannical.

Chinese “Raids.”
The anti-Chinese deputation which recently
waited upon Premier Parkes to urge the sup-
pression of the Mongolian fan-tan industry
made about as large a mistake as any one depu-
tation could well do. According to the evidence
brought forward, there are something like 400
yellow speculators in Sydney alone, who live
exclusively by running various seductive
games ; and in one end of a leading street no
less than thirty-six “ hells ” are in full blast
about six or seven days in the week. Further,
it is claimed that the police receive liberal tips
from the proprietary heathen, and thereby the
way of Ah Sin is made smooth, and he is enabled
to lead his patrons in green pastures without
interference. This was the case advanced by
the deputation, and on the strength of it the
said deputation demanded that “raids” should
be made with greater frequency in order that
the idolater might become dispirited and aban-
don his nefarious occupation. It would have
been a great deal more to the point if it had de-
manded that raids, as the term is at present
understood, should be entirely abandoned, now
and for evermore. There is no more sense in
raiding a Chinese gambler than there is in raid-
ing a larrikin or a burglar, and if the former
individual were treated with the same Spartan
brevity as the others he would be captured a
great deal oftener. The difference between the
methods employed is just the reason why the
Mongol flourishes. When a vigorous and in-
telligent policeman hears six rowdies rioting in
a hotel, he simply rushes in and seizes the
first one he can get hold of, and as many
more as he can capture with both hands, and it
is a cold day when he fails to secure at least
two law-breakers by this simple and effective
process. If he came across thirty-six such riots
in a day, he would probably rush in and get
somebody every time. But the same officer
wanders daily past the thirty-six fan-tan estab-
lishments already mentioned, and though he
knows that the law is being broken in every
one of them, he doesn’t rush in or capture
anybody. Apparently, too, his superiors
never even ask why he allows his beat to
be crowded up with criminals, without any
interference on his part. But, about once in a
long time these officials organise a massive
expedition, which looks as if it were going to
storm a besieged city, and send it out against
the enemy. It takes weeks to get it up, and
while it is preparing, there is an air of bustle
and dark, piratical excitement about the depart-
ment which gives the whole proceeding away.
Also, there are paragraphs in the evening
papers, and only the most thick-headed of
Mongols can fail to take warning. The hollow
tramp of the expedition can be heard two streets
away, and the proceedings are about as con-
spicuous as a torchlight procession, accompanied
by bonfires and blazing kerosene, and banners
and a brass band, and when the great cara-
van gets fairly started, it is generally about
a month too late to capture anybody. The
burden and physical exhaustion of the movement
make it practically impossible to get up more
than one raid in ten weeks without wearing
holes in the department, and in between times
the Chinaman follows his calling in peace. The
only possible conclusion is that there is too
much style about the campaign. A Chinaman
is easy to capture —much easier, as a rule, than
the smallest of Aryan drunks, and he is twenty
times less formidable than even a dwarf bur-
glar. If they went home and organised them-
selves, and laid in provisions for a campaign,
and called out the reserves, when these pecu-
lators were about, they would only capture
about one prisoner per annum ; as it is, the
force jumps upon them whenever they show
themselves, and takes them without any cere-
mony. The same process would suit the fan-tan
sharp admirably. If the passing constable
rushed his premises in a plain, disorganised,
rough-and-ready fashion every time he had
reason to believe the law was being broken
inside—which is probably about 38 times a day
—and collared any law-breaker he could get
hold of in the confusion, just as lie does with
other kinds of criminals, he would produce
more effect than 150 years of the present
elaborate system. If he got one Mongol in six
tries, on a steady average, the fan-tan business
would fall off in less than a week. This is an
exceedingly simple remedy, but apparently no
one ever thought of it before. The Chinese
Anti-Gambling Association doesn’t want more
frequent raids—what it wants is one raid that
will last all the time. And as the law provides
for this, the deputation might reasonably have
asked Parkes what had become of the law, and
why it wasn’t enforced ?

Railway Revelations in Victoria.
The new yearning for political purity in
Victorian railways management is alleged to
be finding expression in the reports of a Stand-
ing Committee, Bent, as arch-angel, in the
chair. For months past, these seekers after
truth have prowled around the wilderness,
absorbing a good deal of refreshment on their
weary way, to collect evidence respecting lines
that run nowhere in particular, and stations
erected for the convenience of rural populations
which don’t exist. Armed with the sorrowful
fact that Commissioner Speight’s estimates arc
constructed on the same commercial principles
as the baseless fabric of a dream—knowing, as
everybody knows, that the sure profit on each
scheme of railway extension always results in a
certain loss—the Standing Committee went
forth to find an explanation that was already
clear as daylight to the community at large.
No picnic pilgrimage was necessary, and the
Standing Committee might as well have stood
on their heads at the top of Collins-street all
the time. But they penetrated into the interior
on a comfortable salary, collecting statistics on
the spot, and ocular proofs at the scene of
action, until at last they felt justified in advis-
ing their bleeding country that the explanation
of the great railway joke was land—L A N D—
land. The previous innocence of Tom Bent
and his colleagues on this otherwise well-known
point, indicates that they must be political
Arcadian shepherds who haven’t read The
Bulletin as much as one could wish. Now
that they know, however, they are quite
alarmingly free with disclosures, and moral
comments thereon, and the Age, in an equally
reckless spirit, quotes as follows from the
Committee’s report:—
In many Instances lines have been surveyed dose to
three chain roads, which might be used as the track for
the railway, thereby saving almost the entire sum esti-
mated by the department for land and compensation.
The Inglewood to Durham Ox line is one of many cases in
point. The country is almost level, and the local bodies
interested have written to the committee stating that
they are quite willing to give portion of the road for the
railway track. Another instance is the proposed railway
from Longwood to Nalinga, where the surveyor of the
department suggested to the Engineer-in-Chief that the
line should be carried along a two-chain main road for a
distance of six or seven miles, giving as his reasons that
there was considerable settlement along it, and a large
traffic, besides room to run the line on it. Notwith-
standing this, he was directed to survey the land at the
side of that road, and on no account to keep on the road.
The above piece of report upon lines yet un-
made throws a little light upon lines already
created and worked at a loss, or rather it
doesn’t throw any light, but merely officially
confirms what the public have long known, or
shrewdly suspected, to be a fact —viz., that the
first consideration in constructing Victorian
railway lines is the purchase of somebody’s land
on terms satisfactory to the vendor. On the
vital question of why land that has been sur-
veyed and estimated to cost a certain amount,
should invariably cost twice as much, the Stand-
ing Committee seem to preserve a dark, signifi-
cant silence. Nor do they report upon the
careful and well-considered folly of the average
Victorian Government in selling at, a low figure,
slices of soil to private individuals in order that •
the Railway Department may promptly buy
them back again at a high figure. No,
indeed. The object of the Committee is to dis-
close just as much information as looks bad for
Speight (a risky policy even at that) without
attempting to explain matters that would look
a deal worse for other people. What the Vic-
torians sigh for is a complete list of the legis-
lators who have personally, or through their
dummies, sold land for railway purposes, or
otherwise profited by the laying down of un-
called for lines. The befooled taxpayer wishes
to know the true history of all land taken up
by this extravagant Department, as well as the
reason why the line which ought to run straight
is generally found to diverge a little to the left
when it isn’t making a semi-circular curve the
other way. And, unless the Standing Com-
mittee can supply this long-felt want, they
might as well sit down, for all the real good
they are likely to do.

Dear Bulletin, —The British and Australian
laws defining insanity leave no medical loop-hole
of escape for a murderer who has never suffered
from concussion of the brain, sustained fits of
dementia, chronic “strangeness of manner,” or
has been preceded by a long family line of
maniacs, whose confinement in padded cells can
be proved to the satisfaction of the Court.
Insanity, according to the bewigged fossil who
first thought it out for the British Empire at
large, or the fossil who last amended the ideas
of his venerable predecessor on the job, is that
condition of mind which prevents a person
from understanding the difference between
right and wrong. It is pretty widely recog-
nised by this time that the commonest form of
insanity, the insanity of intelligent people, is a
lack of will-power to resist an impulse which
the mind admits at the time to be foolish, rash,
cruel, or downright wicked —the sort of impulse
which seizes us, as Charles Dickens said,
when we look over the edge of a precipice, and
sorely tempts us to fling ourselves into space.
Every imaginative man has to struggle against
impolitic impulses, of which the sleepy old
law, and its equally stupid idolaters, know
nothing. Murderer Colston, ex-soldier, is an
imaginative man, a highly-intelligent fellow,
who has acquired a taste for slaughter in
good Queen Victoria’s service. His only
conceivable reason for the Narbethong
murder is set forth in his story of the crime. A
horrible old couple had concocted a miserable
scheme to blackmail him, and whilst the hus-
band was threatening to charge him with a
criminal assault upon the hag of his bosom,
Colston killed him. Then Colston “thought
of the old woman,” he says, and went back to
the cottage and killed her. The fury of this
being spent, he became sane as other people
and made himself scarce forthwith. If “general
paralysis of the insane ” means occasional
paralysis of the impulse-resisting power, then
Colston has no doubt got it badly. But, as
half-a-dozen ridiculous doctors state that the
complaint in question is idiocy and raving mad-
ness rolled into one, why, nothing can be
plainer than the fact that Colston hasn’t got it,
except the further fact that a sensible defence
on the mad theory lines might—not as a matter
of law, but as a matter of grace on the part of
the Executive and public opinion—have saved
him from being judicially murdered at an early
date.—Yours, E.F.

ENROLMENT FEE-ONE SHILLING.
Mr. Crick’s Legal Professions-Araalgama-
sion-Bill now before N.S.W. Parliament, provides
shat forensic costume need not be worn by any
lawyer when appearing before a judge.
Cole, the youth sentenced to seven years’
imprisonment for committing 400 forgeries in-
volving £B5OO, a'l of which money he duly
pocketed from the Commercial Banking Co., of
Sydney, began forging at 18 years of age, and
succeeded so well that he was able to keep a stud
of racing-ponies at the expense of the bank.
Dr. A. J Beehag, a brilliant young Sydney
medico, has died of typhoid fever. He had been
but two years in practice and yet enjoyed an
income of at least £5OOO. The doctor had in him
a very strong spice of originality; was a most
excellent operator, and had achieved excellent
results by the use of menthol in pthisis. He had
allowed himself to run down by overwork, and so
could not fight the fever when it came. The death
of such a man is a national loss.

810 Macquarie-strect North, Sydney, August 12,1891.
My Dear Moorarinua,—
The Bernhardt boom is over, and the cantankerous
“pusson ” who could do those things ever so much better
herself finds herself justified in get ting out the little bow and
arrow. For instance, rather a boring feature was the
way Sara used to iterate that tomato-tinted top thatch ; it
made all her women look alike —“ Frou-Frou ” might have
been “Fedora” to a deaf person who had lost
his programme. It is Sara’s pride and joy that
she never wigs it, only adding long plaits and
frizzles to her not too luxuriant thatch ; but the audience
gets tired of sorrowing with a sempiternal strawberry-
blonde. If they don’t, why does that wily comic opera-
manager cork the locks of a fourth of the chorus?
Another thing that made you think Sara’s prices rather
high was the inherent ghastliness of her make-up—on the
stage only -for off it she monkeyed with the cold cream
in a highly successful manner. There were phases of it,
certainly, but her cheek was always too chromotropish for
a nation bred up to the neurotic pale greens and yellows
of the Australienne. But I doubt if anybody anywhere
ever had ears the colour of raspberry-jam, vermilion
inner nostrils, melon-pink temples,, and fingers radish-
colour to the second joint. Apart from the make-up,
what further shook the devotion of the dress-suited
portion of the throng (by the way, in Sydney Sara was
emphatically a woman’s actress) was the widening of the
view when she turned her heels on the house. It was the
sort of prospect one would like to see the moon rise
behind.
Finally, 1 am distinctly sorry Bernhardt ever came to
Australia. Don’t you think it rather a pity such a
meteor should come to dazzle a simple and joyous com-
munity who took Brown-Potter for a histrionic immortal ?
Now they will sit and yawn and grumble at the people
who had them barn-dancing round the circle with two-
guinea bouquets in other times. If I were an “emotional
lead” I would leave tears and long trains severely alone
until Sara is utterly forgotten. Then treble recalls and
gushing notices may once more be the guerdon of the
lady who tangles her feet among the furniture and col-
lides with “ .Mario” instead of shaving him.
Black and mournful printer’s ink is the best thing to use
in embalming the last Australian appearance of Bern-
hardt, which look place at Sydney Royal on Blh inst.
before a sort of John Lucas house. “Camille” was the
bill with the agony at high-pressure, and the audience
scarcely drawing its breath, but 1 couldn't help smiling
at one thing. If somebody whispered or coughed or
dropped an aitch the whole beehive of a house rose and
“ hus-s-shed ” them down and made ever so much moie
row than the cause of it. Some of the women present
bad combed up for the final. Mayoress Manning, in prim-
rose with a cream lace scarf ; Mrs. Forbes Angus in a
picturesque yellow frock ; two sisters in yellow reminded
one vaguely of “this way to the canary-show;” a red-
faced woman aggravated her complexion by a bright
blue silk ; a stagey black velvet and white, trimmed with
gold-jewelled stuff, had a good glittering position in the
front row, and a legal gentleman’s sister in a box nearly
tipped over on to the stage as she stood up and peered
through her long-handled pince-nez at the brass bedstead
of act last.
After excited recalls and a greater deluge of floralities
than I have ever before envied (including the inevitablefloral
ship, which strikes me as being in such execrable taste-
something like handing a visitor his hat) the curtain
descended and J.C.VV. came forward with a beam-
ing smile to announce that Bernhardt would be visited
on the stage by a number of the profession, and when the
rag rose there they all were- Myra Kemble, Pattie Brown,
Elton, Bracy, Anson, Brough, Geo Rignold, W. ditto—
also a übiquitous old lady whose eldest child ha s
lately burst forth as a two-line slavey, and who regards
herself as histrionic in consequence, I suppose. Every
actor and actress of the “there’s so-and-so” sort was
staged round Sara and a French flag gently waved at
every corner of the proscenium, yards of stuff decora-
tions, showers of flowers, and the howls of the mesmerised
gallery, made the closing scene a wild, cleverly-staged-
managed enthusiasm, you bet.
After that, a large procession ambled behind the
scenes, where Sara, in the white muslin robe of the last
act, leant on a French doctor’s shoulder, and was mobbed
by her fond admirers. It was a marvel the Star didn’t
whirl in her mental orbit, for the hot, gaseous air, the
mingling odors of paint, flowers, fifty different perfumes
in the dressing-room was enough to make a strong man
falter where he stood ; but the Divine one seemed rather
to like it. Much kitten French and gushing buggery was
gone through, but the spectacle of all was when the
dapper, little, nose-glassed critic of a pious morning paper
(who is understood to be quite a harmless tame cat) was
encouraged by the doctor aforesaid to kiss Madame on
the cheek. From what I saw of it, the lady didn’t return
it, though she did put her fair arms round his neck—and
it is, therefore, highly probable that the last “ notice ” of
that recorder will contain more delirium to the square
foot than any of its predecessors. Sara got into her little
bed at the Australia when the cocks began to crow.
“The Profligate,” at Sydney Garrick, seemed a strange
erotic symptom in which the heroine looked as if she
wanted to eat off the same plate as the hero all the time,
and rushed about after him with her mouth open in a
hopelessly mashed manner that must have made the mar-
ried portion of the audience wish they were dead accord,
ingly. But Olga Nethersole dressed Mrs. Profligate
engagingly. In the first act a bridal sort of frock in cream
silk and insertion with puffed sleeves and flat hat trimmed
with yellow roses— it is nice to see a floral hat at this
period—it reminds one of the flowers that bloom in the
spring, tra la. Act 2, pink pongee with flounce of cream
Valenciennes and girdle, and pulled sleeves of pink
chiffon ; collars of cream lace and floral h&t in pink roses.
In this act the graceful Miss Stonehay comes out in a
figure which would benefit by a little dressmaker's art. I
certainly abhor a woman who makes an impromptu hosc-
and-handkerohief satchel out of her bodice every time she
goes out, but when a lady is what one may call shingle-
chested she ought certainly to manufacture an undula-
tion, otherwise the audience can never tell whether she
has got her back to them or not. In act 3 Olga wears a
beautiful dress in ivory silk, the front draped in white
chiffon designed in applique pansies ; sleeves of puffed
chiffon with pansies at the top ; Tosca sash of pansy
velvet, and a sort of Fiji apron around the front of pansy
flower-stalks and leaves. Act 4, a green velvet robe with
sleeves, yoke and petticoat (slightly revealed) cf fawn
green, making rather a picturesque tout. Of the other
people, Cautley and “ Wilfred Murray ’ looked and posed
exactly like superior shop walkers, and the betrayed
damsel was the fac simile of Lady X. There was a full-
dress house on the evening I was present, including
Virginia Earle in an infantile get-up aggravated by daffo-
dils.
What a lot of feathers they are wearing on the Block !
T 13 women I saw there on Saturday morning were all fat
and feathers.
A sort of April occasion in Sydney was the departure of
the Himalaya for England with the time-expired crews of
the Orlando, Curacoa, Cordelia, &j.—because there were
tears for the travellers and sweet, sweet smiles for
their substitutes. The naval gods made quite a little
festival of it, and a large number of people (princii ally
women, need I add?) boarded the Or'ardo at “ Ilis Ex-
cellency ” Admiral Scott’s invitation to sea the last cf the
Mohicans, so to speak. Lady Charles Scott was present,
as was every other woman who could manage to get there
anyhow, and the mixed up sentiment of the ocoas'on was
soothed by coffee and crackers in the saloon of the flag-
ship. By the way, it was simply lovely to see the way in
which a sad-faced girl, who had looked ready to weep in
her little ball cf a lacs handkerchief while the Himalaya
got under weigh, skipped down among the fiist flight for
the cake-section, altho’ the troop-ship was sti 1 gliding
mournfully out of the harbour.
Talking about naval men, there are some women who
don't love them, and there are fond mammas who like to
keep Matilda always within coo-ee, and object to her
pairing with a maritime person, because he is hustled
about so from pillar to post. One woman whose child
yielded to the entreaties of a gay sea-dog, almost weeps
over the fact that her daughter’s husband was so long away
that their three-year-old sonlet calls him “ that man.”
There is no mistake about the trail of the bill-sticker
being over us all. They 7 send notices to the dailies now
when a girl gets her to a nunnery. Says the D.T.: —“ At
St Bridget’s, Jambn’oo, last Sunday, Miss O’Brady (in
religion Sister Theodosia Mary Magdalen), daughter of Mr.
E. O’Brady, Croagingolong, was received into the order cf
“Our Lady of Mercy.” The next thing, 1 suppose, will
be a thrilling account of the robbery of Mother So-and-
so’s antique rosary, when the rival convent has boomed
itself by letting the papers know that the inmates are all
heiresses and wear silk stockings bought with their own
money.
East Sydney Rowing Club ball, at West's Rooms, on
7th instant, was one of the most successful the club has
held—quite seventy couples were present. W. Hamlin
and H. Ackland were hon. secs., and from first to last
kept the ball a-roiling by providing partners for the
partnerless and otherwise doing the guardian angel in a
dress suit. Two sisters, in white silk with pretty
bouquets, looked well; so did a sea-green spotted net
trimmed with gold ; cream, with long art draperies of
yellow, edged with chiffon frills, suited a married lady ;
and a cream brocade, edged with gold braid, would have
been all serene if the girl hadn’t made it herself. Some
crossed oars added to the decorations on the dais—but
why did they put up a pair of sculls with no “ buttons ”
on them. They didn’t look “ professional”—not a bit.
Speaking; of sales—a plethora of which always occurs in
August—l am told that at a big one in Sydney an
went about warning the shoppers to look after their bags
and purses. Quite English, you know, wasn’t it? And
yet, after all, the greatest pocket danger lay in the beau-
tiful young lady behind the counter who was pushing a
mildewed line in her best manner and draping a damaged
parcel of plush so that the spots wouldn’t show.
Quite a gay circle (including a Yaucluse theatre party)
assembled at the Criterion, the other night, to giggle at
the Frenchy funniments of “ Dr. Bill ” with Myra Kemble
playing the frisky Mrs. Horton in several nice frocks.
Myra’s seraphic form has cast off the plumpness of in-
activity, and she looks charming all along—especially in
the silk tea-gown with chiffon sleeves and girdle of sage-
green and gold guipure in which she flabbergasted the
Brown family. And, talking of the second act, when
“ Dr. Bill ” was in Brisbane, uninitiated scene-shifters
were called in on one occasion when the others were on
strike. In the middle of the second act, as the maid was
fishing for the key among Mrs. Horton’s corsets, down
came the rag with a run. “ Why did you let the curtain
down?” fumed Myra to the blushing “frauds.” “Why,
mum,” came the response, “ we thort it was time, as you
was beginning to take your clothes orf.” By the way,
one can’t help smiling at Pattie Brown’s kangaroo-dance,
although that quicksilver little lady is suffering from a
complicated Latin knee complaint in three syllables, and
ought not to be on the gay fantastic toe.
In happy tadpole times, when the rain descends and
the mud riseth up to meet them, one can’t help being
struck by the feeble grip with which the average woman
hitches her pendant draperies out of harm’s wet way.
Half the back of her gown is grabbed high and dry,
certainly, and then the other half is left hanging loosely,
swabbing up all the liquid increment round about, and
the last state of that woman is worse than her first. But
one hardly blames her—for there are only two ways of
keeping one’s final frill spotless in bad weather, and
neither of them looks nice enough to be photographed in.
One is to take off your skirt and hang it in a dry place ;
the other is to give both hands to the job, and trust to
the chivalry of your male friends to look another way as
you loom across the gutter. To keep a clean keel and
look pretty in the rain, is about as impossible as to fall
up a hill.

I wonder if a scribbler ever realises the awful danger
there is in trying to get too far off the beaten track of
every-day English’? For instance, recently I turned up a
country paper in which the reporter, “doing” a local
wedding, ambiguously commences; "Monday was the
day Mr. Smith and Miss Brown elected to jump over the
broomstick,” &c. The spectacle of a broomstick jumping
over the reporter was the probable result of this little
verbal eccentricity’, don’t you think?
“ Adoption as a fairy-tale means of sneaking into an
unborn somebody’’s inheritance isn't quite the rosy game
it used to be in the good old days. Mr. James Swan,
M.L.C., of Brisbane, who died at Suez (en route), the
other day’, left the whole of his self-made £25,0C0 to a
cold-water religious sect, provided they band over £SCO
a year for life to his young and disconsolate widow, but
never willed even the historical shilling to his adopted
daughter whom he made one of his family in 1860, or
thereabouts. Perhaps her marriage put her out of the
race—at any rate, the lady probably regards “ adoption ”
with a strong expression at present.
On dit, that at a recent “society” Sydney assembly
two ycung ladies were informed by the stewards that
unless they danced “in a different manner ” they would
be hustled into their wraps and sent homo. More of the
emancipation of women, probably. Man has hugged her
long enough in the waltz—now she is beginning to hug
him.
Melbourne is all agog over the “Japanese wedding’*
just now—the heroine of which is to be Miss Alice
Speight, a daughter of the Victorian Railway Coramis
sioner, and the bridegroom Mr. Baker, a widower. Miss
Speight set sail for Japan the other day with a costly
trousseau and cases of gifts, many’ of which were, I am
told, of very Yokohamaish character, foolishly enough, as
she will have quite enough of that sort of thing when she
gets there. By the way, Mr. “ Dick ” Speight is affianced
to Miss Fysh, daughter of the Tasmanian Premier. From
this it seems that the Speights don't find any (matri-
monial) prophets in (heir own country.
It did seem a reversal of things to see Bernhardt in
front at Her Majesty’s on Monday evening at the “ Merry
Wives of Windsor,” played in the usual soothing style by
Bill Rignold and brother George, assisted by the company.
There was a good house. Sara, accompanied by as many
people as could possibly crowd in whether invited or not,
occupied the vice-regal box, adorned with tri colour, not
plants, and a huge managerial corbeille, which balanced
on the box-ledge, while the stalls thought of putting up
umbrellas as a guarantee of good faith. “She” looked
fagged-out, but still charming, in a chrome-green velvet
and pale-pink chiffon gown, the high collar and front
edged with green feathers—two bunches of pink rose-
buds at neck and waist ; the long wrinkled gloves
to which she gives her name reaching past the
elbow, cream Manola cloak with the high gold
broidered collar lined with pink feathers. The liom ss
behaved quite rationally, clapping Harcourt ecsta'
tically for his Dr. Cains, because of the Frenchyness of the
part, and nursing a small child in a long frock which Dar-
mont brought to her for, a whole act, to the rapture cf the
galleryites, at whom Sara aimed a divine smile once o r
twice, as if she rather fancied the Australian gods. Du-
quesne, squatting on the floor of the box, fetched and
carried all the evening- first a cushion and then a fur-rugi
and a blonde masher faltered into The Presence with
much pantomime and a big bouquet, which Sara seized
by the centre in a way which must have hurt hisfeelings.
I couldn’t help feeling cruelly glad as Sara and the rest of
les belles frongsays appeared to be straining themselves
to know w’hat was going on on the stage—it was such a
quid pro quo. They tortured us with their lightning
French long enough—it was quite the boot on the other
foot.
At the close of the second act Handsome George, in the
picturesque heliotropie velvet suit of “Master Ford,’
stepped forth and speechified, asking for three cheers for
Bernhardt, which were shrieked with three cheers for
himself thrown in. Then the Marseillaise was played,
and everybody perpendicularised, and Darmont, standing
up beside Sara, sang the words in a boisterous baritone,
and somebody cried “ Hurroo ” from the gallery, and
Bernhardt undulated her thanks. In fact, there was
nearly as much irresponsible gush as on the last night at
the Royal, when people wept all over her dressing-room,
and George Anson (he speaks French) tripped in ahead
of several eager cavaliers and led Sara B. out to her
kerridge —Comus and Melpomene arm-in-arm.
Yours affectionately,
SAPPHO SMITH.

The Bal Masque at Cocgee Aquarium, on Slst ultimo,
was attended by about four hundred people, mostly in
fancy costumes. But a brace of South Sea Islanders, in
genuine “tappa” and “ sisi ” costumes, looked best
among the men. Only a few wore masks.
Some of the “democratic ratepayers” of Broken-hill
are prancing because the local mayor invited Gov. J. and
other itinerant vice-regalities to a banquet, paid for by
the town, and didn’t bid the whole community to the
feast, but made the turkey-struggle as “select” as
heaven. But the question is, does a real democrat ever
yearn to take a potato out of the same tureen as the
Governor?

This is the cold and halt-way month
When winter changes airs,
And the dry-goodsman without vain brag
Marks sixpence more on every rag
Of his “ sale-price ” counter wares.
This is the time when drapers nice
Push the moth-eaten bale,
And quote the old original price,
As “ such a reduction,” “ a sacrifice,”
And thus women crowd to the sale !

After giving 28 performances in Mel-
bourne seven in Adelaide, and 32 in Sydney, the
of’ Sara’s genius dipped beneath the Aus-
tralian theatrical horizon with a lurid glow that
resembled the iridescent gleam of her own
Titianesque looks. (N.8.-This is a feeble attempt
to emulate the soulful flapdoodle of the S.M.
Herald.) Bernhardt business, fading away at
Svduey Royal, came with a rattle during the last
three nights, and “Camille,” the closing repre-
sentation, was witnessed by a Large and
enthusiastic crowd who cheered themselves
hoarse at the end of every act—till the
scene-shifters must have got sick of hauling-up
and letting-down the curtain—while hurling on
flowers with an expedition and inaccuracy of
aim that betokened more zeal than discretion. At
the close, after three calls, the audience remained
at the request of James Cassius, and the curtain
being again drawn-up, Sara was seen surrounded
by her company and all the leading lights of the
Australian theatrical world. The band at the
rear struck up “The Marseillaise,” the French
flag was wildly waved from the proscenium
boxes, and the audimoe yelled wildly, while
half-a’-dozen gentlemen, including the handsome
Georges, Rignold and Anson, conveyed floral
tributes of fanciful design harps, crowns,
wreaths, ships, &c.—to the feet cf
world’s greatest dramatic genius. Trie
curtain was then lowered to allow Kowalski to get
ready for a bright little speech which he subse-
quently fired off with great effect, but the lowered
curtain had again to be drawn up in response to
unanimous appeals for Sara, which came from a
house that climbed up on seats and hung over
railings, regai’dless of appearances. It was nearly
twelve when proceedings terminated, and then
the “ God-given janius ” retired from the Royal
stage for the last time to her carriage, escorted by
the gallant Anson, who bore the belle away, ’mid
the frowns of her envious compatriots. She sailed
in the Mariposa on Tuesday.
** * *
Some excitement was caused at Her
Majesty’s during the later nights of “ Honest
Hearts and Horny Hands ” by the action of
Duncan B. Harrison, who came before the cur-
tain and condemned, in no measured terms, the
attitude espoused by the Sydney press towards
his show. Long ere the opening night, he
said, it had been prejudged and condemned.
One newspaper proprietor, averred Harrison,
had given his employes instructions to damn
it, because he had wagered £lOO that he
could ruin any American play that came along,
while the editor of another paper had
ordered his subordinates to cut it up without
mercy. These statements were received with
solemn silence from the house at the time, and by
the newspaper press subsequently. We trust, for
the sake of peace, that no act ; “n promoting
lawyers were present.
* * *
Sydney D.T. declares that “Jimmy”
Williamson’s good-natured accession to the request
of Sara, on the score of fatigue, to be absolved
from visiting Brisbane, cost her managers at least
£2OOO. Bosh ! It is scarcely likely that seven
performances at Brisbane would have realised a
greater sum than the £4OOO said to have been
taken during a similar period at Adelaide. Against
this amount may be set the time lost through, and
in, travel, say, six days, the transit expenses of
scenery and company, and the sum actually taken
in Sydney in the interval. Viewed without
reference to the coin that might have been taken
by the Opera Co., probably no greater sum than
what was taken by Bernhardt, it is certain that
the Brisbane trip would not have altered the
exchequer to the extent of one maravedi.
* # #
Now Sara has gone it will be easier to
estimate better, not only her worth, but that, in a
lesser degree, of her company. For herself, who
will ever forget the soul-stirring pathos of the
cry “Armand !” as the returned lover approaches
her death-bed ; her anguish of feature, gesture,
and voice as Tosca in the torture scene ; the snarl,
like that of a cornered dog, with which she
strikes down Scarpia in the succeeding scene, and
all the wonderful details that precede her weird,
panther-like exit from the chamber of horrors, lit
up only by the death-candles that glimmer feebly
in the pallid dawn ? As for the company : None
have claim to any absolute excellence, but all are
artists, aiming, not at individual distinction, but
at general ensemble. As an instance in point,
the messenger, with a one-line speech, who
delivers Marguerite’s letter to Armand in the
cottage retreat. One thing about Sara is notice-
able, her performances are never mechanical or
stereotyped. She never played Camille or Floria
Tosca, during her Sydney stay, in exactly the
same fashion. Broadly speaking, of course, the
performance was the same, but in little things it
differed, in bye-play, in stage movements, in the
emphasis of trifling phrases, and so on.

“The Profligate’s ” sowing of wild oats is
said to have been reaping a fat harvest at the
pay-boxes of Sydney Garrick. Consequently, he
continues to flood the auditorium with moral
aphorisms and tears until Saturday night, when
“ The Idler ” follows. “ The Profligate ” will be
played for the last time on the afternoon of that
day. “ A Scrap of Paper ” is said to be in
rehearsal.
* * * *
Despite the fact that admission to the
gallery was two shillings, a house, crowded in
every part, greeted with tumultuous applause the
return of Billy Elton, Charles Ryley, the “tiny-
tots,” and Henry Bracy with the Opera Co., at
Sydney Royal, on Monday last. “ Old Guard,”
musically considered, is a feeble “Paul Jonesy ”
rehash, with an ill-developed plot and no situa-
tions worth speaking off. It only contains one
character, thePolydore of Elton, that is not afeeble
replica of some type we've seen hundreds of times.
All the others are simply silhouettes, dim outlines
of old, worn-out stereos, that have neither height,
breadth, depth, nor rotundity. Therefore is
Elton, as was Arthur Roberts, at London Avenue
three years ago, the whole show and the dog
under the waggon. “ Old Guard ” is fairly well-
dressed, well-scened, thanks to Goatcher and
Brunton, and its orchestration is excellent. So is
the stage-drilling, though, bar the Fin-de-Siefe
ballet —for whose arrangement Marie Reddall
took a weU-deserved call on opening night—
there is little dancing and less marching.
The “opera” needs a heap of building-up,
and Elton—made up cranially to resemble “Bill
Nye”— not only lays the bricks, but carries the
hod, albeit his magnificent vocal organ was a
little husky on the premiere. Ida Osborne has
seceded, and her place is filled by Julia Loel, a
young lady of pleasing presence, with a mezzo-
soprano of good quality, but wanting in smooth-
ness and flexibility. Alongside her, Florence
Young, much-improved, appears a capable actress.
Several numbers have caught on, particularly
Elton’s “Dashing Militaire,” Ryley’s “When
Thou Art Near,” an introduced song composed by
Conductor Caron, and “Farewell, my Humble
Home,” sung with much taste by Julia Loel.
se * * *
It is a long leap from Harrison and John
L. Sullivan to Shakespeare and Bill Rignold, but
Sydney Her Majesty’s Co. cleared the chasm on
Monday night, without straining anything. The
“Merry Wives” is played by the original cast,
except in the case of Master Slender, whose gar-
ments are now filled by Mr. Ivimey, a feeble copy
of Harry Hoyte, their previous wearer. William
Rignold’s fat knight has even gained in breadth
and unctuousness with age and sea-voyaging,
and Ford, played by Brother George contrary
to report, is of its old-time excellence.
Messrs, Jewett, Harcourt, Sweeney, Greena-
way Burford, Hazlitt, Whyte, Rigby, and
the Misses Bishop, Watts-Phillips, Rignold,
and Tracey repeat former successes, and the only
leading feature not up to the mark of twelve
months past is the fairy dance of the last act.
Madame Bernhardt, some of her company, and a
few hard-shelled social limpets were in and around
the vice-regal box all through on Monday night,
and applauded throughout right lustily. Between
acts G. R. came ,to the front and called on the
house to arise and cheer, with might and main,
the great woman. After everybody had
responded, the band struck up the Marseillaise,
in which everybody, including Sara and Darmont,
joined with Gallic energy and enthusiasm. Thus
was ushered in the “Merry Wives’ ” revival, thus
closed Sara’s last night in Australia.

with a great actress, what sort of criticism can
you reasonably expect? You can easily see that
Nugent Robertson never kissed Sara.

Madame Simonsen’s new Italian Opera
Company opened the attack at Melbourne
Alexandra on Saturday night. With fashion
fully represented in the dress-circle, crowded
stalls, every inch of standing space occupied in
the old shilling pit, and a gallery well nigh loaded,
the appearance of the house—cleaned and done up
for the occasion—was an entertainment in itself.
Moreover, the display of musical enthusiasm
almost equalled the furore of four-and-a-half years
ago, when the first Simonsen company sang the
same opera on the same boards. Saturday’s per-
formance of “II Trovatore ’’ was in some respects
superior to the one by which it was judged,
though, perhaps, less satisfying to the average
unculchawed listener, who mostly swears by the
soprano’s solos, and is easily taken captive by a
tenor’s top-note. Right away it became evident
that Napoleone Maffezzol, the conductor, is a
first-class general, able to make the very most of
a sufficiently large orchestra, and especially strong
in his control of a limited chorus. Such a sharp,
clean, neat rendering of the opera throughout
has probably not been heard before in Melbourne.
It was a credit to the chorus-master and a genuine
triumph for the commander-in-chief, who, by the
way, put things through with an air of
composure that is far from being the badge
of all his tribe. The principals—as many
of them, at least, as showed on Satur-
day—run strictly on traditional lines. The
prima-donna, Guidotti, weighs a few stone more
than her tenor adorer, Signor Vilalta, whilst the
jealous baritone of the evening, Signor Sisco,
wears hair all round his face, and could also sit on
the tenor if the plot of the narrative gave him a
chance. The baritone’s white cloak (a preposter-
ously thin and raggy garment), the tenor’s
humiliating helmet, and the soprano’s tragic black
dress are just what was expected, also the way in
which they all rush up and down the stage or bolt
off at a certain given bar of music are pathetic
reminders of the dead, dim past. Their readiness
to come forward and bow acknowledgements in the
middle of a supreme moment of passion is another
well-remembered feature of the lyric drama. In
fact, the general resemblance between the event of
Saturday last and its Alexandra original was as
striking as the likeness which one yard of
maccaroni bears to another 36 inches of that
. farinaceous food. The reappearance of Cavalleri
as the gipsy assisted to make many of theaudience
feel a few years younger. Time has improved the
fine contralto voice of Signorina 0., besides
giving another touch of power to her
always efficient playing of the part. Sisco
is a good melodious baritone with a more
free, easy, and accessible sort of walk than his
predecessor, Pirnazzoni, whom he otherwise re-
sembles. lorio, the basso, seems promising, and
two or three subordinate characters throw off their
little bits of warble like artists. Signorina Gui-
dotti is a nice-faced, buxom prima-donna and
emotional actress, with a high, forced soprano,
less powerful than Ciuti’s was, but more sym-
pathetic in the middle notes. The other lady,
without question, could shriek to greater advan-
tage in the concerted numbers. Vilalta is merely
a good specimen of the average Italian tenor who,
strictly speaking, doesn’t sing tenor at all. Vil-
alta pumps up a metallic flow of falsetto, which
s in tune without being melodious. However,
his voice, although sadly unsympathetic and
strained, will probably be found to wear well, and
it certainly attains to a high altitude in its own
weird, peculiar way. Should several other artists
(who were to appear in public ere this) turn out
superior to the first samples it is very likely that
the season will boom. Melossi, a baritone of big
repute, was due as the Count di Luna on Tuesday,
together with a second basso. On Wednesday
and Thursday (to-night) the “ star,” Signora Cut-
tica, appears with her tenor husband in “Martha.”
If the Signora’s voice is anyway sweet, “Martha”
ought to knock ’em, for concerning the lady’s
vocal powers there is said to be no possible doubt
whatever.
