At the Opera House they were to close the
“County Fair” after Tuesday, and shunt that
dreadful, noisy machine which used to work Cold
Molasses first past the post by a short nose. For
the three following nights the theatre remains
closed, in order that the eminent fighter-actor,
J. L. Sullivan, may be put into rehearsal and pro-
duced in something like a common-sense manner
on Saturday next (15th). It is rumoured and
hoped that the village blacksmith will not sport
evening dress to make a horseshoe in.
* * * *
“ Miss Laura Yilliers and her entire com-
pany from London ” are beginning to look very
lonesome-like amidst the regilded vastness of
Melbourne Royal. Even with the adventitious
aid of a newspaper-controversy, it was found
impossible to prop up “ The Dancing Girl ” for a
third week, and on Saturday that badly-treated
nymph, likewise the dear dook and his humorous
auntie, took themselves off in favour of a double
bill, “We Two” and “ Woodbarrow Farm.”
Seeing that the public showed little desire to
back the double bill, and seeing furthermore that
it wasn’t accepted with much gusto, the reason
why Miss Yilliers and Co. don’t give themselves
up as a bad job will soon require as much finding
as the North Pole. In “ The Dancing Girl ” they
fell below expectation with a sickening thud,
and the change of programme leaves them
gasping for appreciation on the old level. They
haven’t come to fill a vacuum, or supply a long-
felt want in Australia. In point of superfluous-
ness, these well-meaning strangers may be com-
pared to a sack of coke at Newcastle, and the
question of what on earth to do with ’em cries
aloud for thoughtful consideration by the Royal
proprietary. The managing-director and friends
ought to form a glee-party behind the scenes, and
sing “ Laura, dear Laura, go. ‘home’ with you
now.” If the lady persists in carrying out her
engagement after that, then they ought to per-
suade her to play something with an American
novelty in it, and give the Hairless Horse from
the Waxworks a leading character. The spectacle
of that unfledged beast of burden romping to
victory in a mechanical race would draw good
money.
* * * *
“ We Two ” is a weak one-act yarn, show-
ing how Miss Yilliers and Clifton Anderson are
drifting apart, or something, mainly in conse-
quence of A If. Boothman, who invades their
domestic circle in tan shoes and an ulster. For-
tunately an old family butler forgets to do some-
thing, or does something which he had forgotten,
after which the clouds roll by, and Boothman’s
tan shoes never darken the other people’s door
again. Anyway, it all comes right, whereas the
acting manages to go a trifle wrong throughout.
The effect produced is a yawn. “Woodbarrow
Farm,” by Jerome K, Jerome, seems a bright
little play, containing many smart lines, two or
more well-sketched conventional characters, and
at least one striking incident. A man who pre-
tends to be somebody else drops dead, and the
real person, who was intended to be murdered,
turns up unexpectedly and is identified as his
buried impersonator. Meantime a noble-hearted
yokel has come into the property by mistake and
been mashed by the wife of the necessary
villain, and when he refuses, for the sake
of his unfortunate attachment, to assist the
man that wasn’t killed in securing the villain,
there is nothing left for the noble yokel
but to return to Woodbarrow Farm and settle
down for good on three acres and a cow. The
drama ends in peaceful smoke, and would not take
the town’s breath away even if it were produced
with a B. and B. cast. Under present conditions
it gives a fairly interesting idea of how much
better the Farm might be worked. Julius
Knight doesn’t carry quite enough guns for the
trying part of the suddenly-enriched country-
man, whilst Miss Yilliers, rather attractively
made-up, again proves that she lacks force and
feeling, rather than experience. Percival Stevens
(the company are great in stage names), who
appeared as Mr. Crake in the withdrawn piece,
makes something of his opportunities in this
instance. The rest are either a little livelier, or
else no better, than they gave promise of being
at the outset of their Australian careers. No-
body, in fact, gets near to revealing any unsus-
pected talent, but Brunton’s scenery is cleverly
performed as usual.
* * & *
The market for Gaiety burlesque remains
firm, and buyers flocked to the Princess’ again
last week. *“Ruy Bias,” however, must posi-
tively draw its gorgeous inanity to a close on
Saturday night, because “ Cinder-Ellen ” will be
ready to kick up her volatile legs on the Monday
following (17th). Sunday, of course, is always set
apart by the Gaiety Company for prayer and
thanksgiving,
* * * *
“Peril,” at Melbourne Bijou, is
liked, especially by the local aristocracy, who
come in glittering relays of theatre-parties to see
the naughty capting chivvy Lady Ormond round
the coal-scuttle. Perhaps they heave a sigh of
disappointment when that virtuous enigma closes
the shutters between herself and the capting with
the pious ejaculation that she is “safe!” but
sassiety must admit that the shutters are a satis-
factory solution of the difficulty. The farcical
comedy incidents sail along a rippling sea of
laughter, and if “ Peril ” were more robustly built
and had a sterner purpose it would fill the pretty
house for a long time. It runs over Saturday pre-
sumably.
* * * *
Halle concerts, despite the excited shrieks
of musical critics, are not in great request at the
Town Hall, although programmes are now
generally described as “ popular ” in the advertise-
ments. The famous artists take a last grand final
farewell on Monday next when a crowded audience
might be expected but for the fact that Halle
farewells seem to come round pretty often.

Frank Clark & Co. flourish at the Gaiety,
also the Vic. pegs along quietly. The Waxworks
is still located at the old spot and the Oyclorama
promises to exchange “Waterloo” for a new
subject—“ Eureka Stockade ” —in a week or two.
Fortunately the Gillies Government is “out,”
otherwise Wrixon would have brought in a bill to
suppress “ Eureka ” on account of its disloyalty tc
the Crown, or something.

The substitution of “ Les Cloches de
Corneville ” for “Bridget O’Brien, Esq.,” at the
Adelaide Boyal, by the Sheridan combination,
has been admittedly a failure. Talented as the
great J. F.’s company are in their own little line,
they are off-colour in opera. Warwick Gainor,
with his robust organ, was, in tome respects, one
redeeming feature of the production, and although
Jules Simonsen sang prettily, he wanted life and
finish. Johnny Sheridan’s make-up as Gaspard
was complete and ghastly enough, but here
remarks should cease. “Fun on the Bristol ”is
being restored to bring matters round. Then, on
the 14th and 15th, Adelaide will be engaged in
discussing a new drama, “The Jonquil,” by a
local author-actor (Mr. Guy Boothby). Wybert
Reeve takes the responsibility of the production,
which will be interpreted by amateurs, assisted
by Harry Norman as coach and stage-manager.
At Garner’s Rooms, Tommy Hudson continues
his brilliant career; and Felix Tanner keeps on
with the 40 days and 40 nights biz.

The Achurch-Charrington crowd did re
markable business at Broken Hill, opening with
“ Forget Me Not,” and changing to “Camille,”
“ Doll’s House,” &c. They were assisted by the
presence in the silver city of Lords Jersey and
Kintore, and the enthusiasm of the inhabitants
over a copious rainfall, and the Theatre Royal was
densely packed every night.
At Poverty Point.
Patti seeks £1000 a night from Abbey and Grau for an
American tour.
A posse of police the other night, about 12 o’clock,
raided a hotel adjoining one of Sydney’s leading theatres,
and there surprised a crowd of the “ boys.” Included
therein were several popular actors, whose wit suggested
some fun at the expense of the others. As the latter
entered, the “accusedat the bar' all commenced jabber-
ing in alleged French, and, on being asked for their
names, gave a long string of Gallic titles. The whole
family of Duval (Claude, Armand, and several others)
were represented, several Comtes were included in the
gang, but the joke reached its climax when a Thespian,
recognised everywhere by his Falstafflan proportions,
returned his baptismal cognomen as Alphonse Pierre
Jean Jaques Le Blanc. Nothing has since eventuated.
During her Australian season Sara Bernhardt got a
cheque for £100 every morning at breakfast and £200 on
any day on which a matinee occured. Nor was this the
whole of her remuneration. It was, by the way, Sara’s fad
to pay her hotel-bill every day—nine guineas.
On Bernhardt’s last Sydney Royal night a souvenir
sheet of music, styled “ Pensee,” was given to every lady
in the house. The music was by Georges Charton, one of
the company, and the words composed by Sara, on board
the Monowai, run thus
On devrait vivre pour aimer et
Mourir de ne plus aimer.
In other words—
We ought to live to love and die,
Not loving any more.
Major Henry C. Dane, the lately-wedded and favourably-
known American lecturer, opens a lecture season at
Sydney Centenary Hall, on Tuesday, 18th August.
Repertoire: “Chinese Gordon,” “American Naval
Battles,” and “ Abraham Lincoln.” W. S. Medoalfe look?
after “the front.”

is now dramatic critic of Sydney D.T.
The very latest about Melba:—“The cloak worn by-
Melba when she appeared as Elsa in ‘ Lohengrin ’ excited
the greatest possible admiration. It was made by M
Worth, and is looked upon as quite a chef d’ oeuvre , The
material is cloth of gold, with fleurs-de-lis embroidered
upon it in white. The border, which is also white, is
thickly studded with pearls, rubies and emeralds. It is
immensely heavy, and two stout pages Svere put on by-
Mr. Harris to carry the train of this gorgeous garment in
the wedding scene ; but, in spite of the two bearers, the
movements of Madame Melba were much impeded as she
moved about the stage.” What a tail our cat has got!
A benefit to Johnny Wallace, the immortal Bambini,
will be arranged in Melbourne directly the heads of the
perfesh come forward with their vote and interest, say ing
“ We are here, dear Duke.” Johnny wants a benefit
badly, they say.
Each individual of the new company at Melbourne
Royal appears to consider that the others are weak— very
weak.
The most astounding thing in the last Melbourne opera
season was when Madame Ciuti was taken ill and could
not play Valentine, in the “Huguenots,” whereupon
Madame Simonsen went through it splendidly, without a
rehearsal, never having done it before.
A “ Professor ” Hoffman, described as “ the greatest
prestidigi-comedian and ventriloquist of the world,” has
been fooling them in Newtown (M.L.) with the prize-
distribution fake.
It took a two-horse carriage to convey Sara’s floral
trophies to the Australia after her farewell performance,
Sara Bernhardt, it is said, is so well pleased with her
bust as executed by E. B. Mackennal, the young Austra-
lian sculptor, that she intends to have it reproduced in
marble, bronze and silver.
Says S.M. Herald of Dr. M’Carthy’s portrait of Sara
Bernhardt as Cleopatra; “The head thrown hack with
regnant grace, whilst the blue eyes, suffused with a love-
look of languid, voluptuous expectancy, gaze rapturously
forward, is instinct with the spirit of the character as
represented by Mine. Bernhardt. The warm carnations of
the cheek and the ruddy gleaming of the fair nut-brown
hair, with the rich yellow of the loose robe relieved by the
heliotrope colour of the jewelled sash, give, in their pro-
fusion of harmonious tints, the required suggestion of the
‘ gorgeous East.’ ” Have all the Herald staff kissed Sara
goodbye ?
What caused all the trouble in the swell concert-com-
pany was the fact that the handsome soprano was guilty
of the unpardonable sin of dressing better and looking
better than her gifted female boss. That story about the
missing trunks is declared to be all flam.
The Alexandra, Melbourne, looks fresh and beautiful in
its new decorations, and electric-light now triumphs at
all the Melbourne plays. Poor Joubert! one says again —
the man who furnished Melbourne with its best theatre,
at the date of its completion, and now it pays as well as
any.
Little Simonsen has gone under—under petticoat guv—
because he made so small a success of his last trip to
Europe, wherefore the gifted missus clutches the reins. He
will just be allowed a little fiddle solo, between the acts.
Maffezzoli, Madame Simonsen’s conductor, is charmed
with the orchestra of 35, gathered for him in Melbourne,
and Harcourt Lee has drilled the chorus. Never had
opera in Australia such a band, the cream of the training
by Cowen and Hamilton Clarke, not to speak of such
enthusiasts, in the past, as Hazon, Zelman, Siede, and
Giorza.

POLITICAL POINTS
One man, one speech. That is what Par-
liament wants,
Arthur Rae, M.L.A. (N.S.W.), would
enfranchise aboriginal women.
“With One Man One Vote, clubs will not
be trumps in East Sydney.”—Orange Leader.
Parkes, soi-disant democrat, has always
advocated open voting in preference to the ballot-
box.
The N.S.W. Labour-party does not want
revenge. It is politically hungry and wants its
dinner.
One-man-one-vote, also One-member-one
seat, likewise One-house-one-day-of-election. One-
person-one-vote to follow.
In the race between monarchies and
republics in the civilised world the former now
claim 26 and the latter 25 nations.
Melbourne Age says that “ M‘Millan’s
abandonment of office at this particular juncture
is viewed as a piece of political cowardice.”
“In politics there is nothing meaner than
to steal without acknowledgment, because the
loser has no redress except public opinion.”—
Reynolds.
Premier Munro says that the Victorian
Government railways have sponged on revenue to
the extent of nine millions sterling over and
above loan-moneys.
The S.A. Ministry are trying to institute
single electorates; all nominations and elections
on the same day. “South Australia leads the
world in democracy.”
The same crowd who put in MTlwraith
hundreds ahead of Griffith for North Brisbane at
last general election can, if they organise, put in
Glassey and a mate at next election.
The Age says that the (Vic ) Assembly
will be contemptible unless it seconds Minister
Shiels in compelling the local Railway Commis-
sioners to publicly vindicate their administration.
Blackall (Q.) Revision Court x’ejected 50
out of 89 applications of citizens to be registered as
voters. The Court consisted of Nixon, P.M., and
station-managers (!) Nullen, Raven, Royds, and
M'Lelland.
London Standard :—“ The Church of
England is allying herself with the true Demo-
cratic spirit, thus cheeking Romanism on one
hand, and Nonconformity on the other.” Ha,
ha ! where is she going to ?
“ Steadfast loyalty to the British Crown is
a prominent characteristic of New Zealanders, as
distinguished from Australians. They never hoot
Queen Victoria’s name in Maoriland,” says a
M.L. paper. So? Why so?
The National Observer, referring to
Lord Salisbury’s African annexations, says “his
achievement is all the more grateful in that it has
been largely bloodless.” Which seems a gentle
substitute for slightly bloody.
There are only 1800 Jews in all Ireland.
Of this number 1074 reside in Dublin, but Clare,
Cavan, Westmeath, and Roscommon can only
boast of one each. What is the use of living
where there’s nothing to pawn ?
Member Donnelly, once a returning*
officer, stated in the N.S.W. Assembly that, prior
to the introduction of the £4O depo-it, bogus can-
didates popped up in shoals, and that on one
occasion a blackfellow was nominated.
In the States, the Farmers’ Alliance (i.e.,
third) party has begun to send members to the
State Legislatures; in England the third (Labour)
party is organising ; in Italy the third party will
contest the next elections. Australia leads the
world.
The “ National ” Association held a meet-
ing in Melbourne last week, when thirty-eight
well-dressed gentlemen rolled up to represent the
nation. And, as far as could be understood, they
were representing the British nation with a great
big B.
Langton comes out of his shell again in
connection with the Victorian “National” Asso-
ciation, to frustrate Labour, in the general election.
What a memory is Edward Langton, of political
verdigris, bitter aloes, calomel! After all, the
monied interest left him poor.
Said a starving young man recently
arrested in London for stealing; “I am not a
thief; I am a Socialist. He is not a thief who,
when workless and necessitous, only takes that of
which he is in need : he only is a thief who,
having enough, takes more.”
The nomadic vote might be taken in N.S.
Wales, it has been suggested, not by transference
of the electoral right to fresh divisions and dis-
tricts, but as in S.A. and Queensland (in the elec-
tion of Divisional Boards under the Local
Government Act) by the transmission of ballot-
papers through the post.
The O'Sullivan, M.L.A., declares that he
obstructs Neild’s Divorce Bill “ on behalf of the
women of N.S. Wales.” On behalf of that section
only whose religion deprives them of the moral,
social, and political right to seek and obtain
divorce when marriage ties are a source of moral,
physical, or intellectual misery.
If the whole of N.S.W. were converted
into one electorate at present, as proposed by some
speakers, with 141 members to be elected out of
perhaps 500 candidates, the returning-officer would
have a lovely task in running down the list to
point out to the illiterate voter the 141 members
whom the latter particularly wished to elect.
M‘Millan’s backers assert that he has paid
off the Dibbs deficit. Yes, in the good old
Parkesian way—-with Treasury bills, or 1.0.U.’5,
not one of which has yet been redeemed. The
deficit still exists. The N.S.W. Treasury is
merely in pawn. We could wipe out all the
national debts in the world ourselves, in that
fashion.
Jawbone Neild proposes by bill in the
N.S.W. Parliament that the holder of every lease
of landed property having a minimum unexpired
currency of 50 years shall be entitled to buy the
fee-simple upon tendering to the owner a sum
equal to 20 years rent, and that he shall have an
action for damages in the event of the owner’s
refusal to surrender the title.
It is suggested that electors be paid £5
apiece to record their votes, so as to induce all, or
nearly all, voters to exercise their right of fran-
chise. The argument is : You pay members for
legislating; why not pay voters for enabling mem-
bers to legislate? The difference is that the man
who votes is only minding his own business ; the
member is minding other people’s.

one end thereof are two ex-judges (£2620 and
£lB2O annual pension respectively), who for long

poor woman who gets £l5 annually for the loss of

some highwayman hath constructed such a pen-

Personal registration of votes should be
supplemented by house-to-house canvas by the
electoral officers’ delegates, and, as has been sug-
gested in the N.S.W. Assembly, public interest
and enthusiasm might be awakened by having
public days of registration. Registration, aye,
and voting, might just as equitably be made com-
pulsory as the registration of births, deaths, and
marriages.
Premier Munro has sent a mi]d protest to
the Indian Government against the influx of our
beloved fellow-subjects, the Indian hawkers, who
roll their kincob eyes so ferociously when a woman
whose husband is out won’t buy But, of course,
in the grand United Empire Ghollah and Chunder
have a right to swarm here by the million. Vic-
torian General Tulloch will tell you how vital
Herat is to Australia.
Copeland, M.L.A. (N.S.W.), objects to
the abolition of the £4O Parliamentary nomination
deposit on the ground that it prevents vexatious
candidature. It does, the vexatious candidature
of the poor man only, for a £4O or £lOOO deposit
cannot prevent the vexatious candidature of the
rich man or his dummy. Monetary deposits in
cases of candidature are simply weapons in the
hands of property-holders.
Premier Munro’s abstention from pro-
posing fresh taxation euchres even the astute
Gillies. Munro absolutely courts a defeat in
Parliament, because a defeat means a dissolution.
And what would Gillies do then, poor thing ? It
would kill him politically to denounce Munro for
not taxing Victorians some more. Gillies yearned
to hear Munro’s budget, in the certain faith that
it would bristle with new taxes.
The present attitude of the Irish priests
towards Parnell reminds The Bulletin of the old
story told of Murphy, who got very “screwed”
one evening at the house of his life-long friend
Flannigan. “ Luk here, Flannigan,” said Murphy
in an outburst of drunken confidence, “ we’ve
been frinds — [hie) —for thirty-siven year, but (con-
fidentially) Oi’ve niver raley liked you, Flanni-
gan.” The Irish hierarchy has gammoned a lot,
but it has never liked Flannigan Parnell.
The Commonwealth Bill is being shunted
by the Tasmanian Parliament to a Federal Con-
vention, consisting of 50 members, elected by the
parliamentary voters. This Convention, to which
members of Parliament may be elected, will be
empowered by statute to consider, and amend, if
necessary, the great measure, which Parkes told
the open-mouthed people of Hobart must not be
touched—not a line or a comma of it.
Scores of workmen want employment in
the Richmond (N.S.W.) district, and along come
a crowd of newchum Austrians who are ready to
sink dams for 10s. a week and food. Scores of
able-bodied workmen are wandering about the
Johnstone River (N.Q.) country, and down come
boatloads of Chinamen from further North who are
eagerly engaged at sweated wages. The Chow has
not yet been half edged out of Australia.
The N.S.W. Cabinet are blowing very cold
on the creation of a Labour Department, now that
they have secured the Labour-party’s support. If
a Department of Agriculture can be created ille-
gally by a Parkes Cabinet, with the tacit conni-
vance of Parliament, why cannot a Department
of Labour be instituted without the passing of a
special bill ? Parkes can be counted on to act
“ hutterly hunconstitutionally if in want of
parliamentary support.
Sydney Echo proclaims in favour of
enfranchising propertied women— which, of course,
would be merely wealth. The
Bulletin wants to know what is "wrong with its
proposal that woman-franchise should first be tried
by enfranchising red-headed women, of whom
some have property and some have not ? As there
are many red-headed Scotchmen, there are, no
doubt, lots of red-headed Scotchwomen. Tims
the Echo’s clientele would get a fair deal.
Kaiser Wilhelm says he will drink no more
wine until he drinks it in France. Wilhelm may
intend a lot, but his threat may not mean much,
after all, Napoleon 111. drank German wine in
Germany—as a prisoner. Wilhelm may yet drink
French wine as an exile enjoying the hospitality
and protection of the Republic. During the Ger-
man occupation of Champagne after the war a
French peasant sowing seeds in a field was jeered
at by a half-tipsy German who said : “ You sow ;
but the crop will be ours.” “That may be so,”
said the Frenchman, “ I am planting hemp.”
In the N.S.W. elections, a defeated Oppo-
sitionist was deprived of his railway-pass within
24 hours of his defeat; while a defeated Minis-
terialist was allowed to continue to travel “on
the nod,” both of them looking for another seat.
The Oppositionist won a seat in the Never Never
country, and the Ministerialist, after being de-
feated a second time, had to pass in his railway-
pass. The question now is—Should the former be
refunded his fares to Bourke, or should the latter
be called on to pay for his free trips out West ?
And a second question is—Are the railways
managed on political aa well aa on commercial
principles ?
Premier Parkes, haring stated in N S.W,
Assembly that he favoured what was styled over-
representation, because it did away with
cliqueism, provided the friction of many minds,
and thus aroused an enthusiasm which sought and
found expression in high-class oral utterance,
Mr. Crick: “Deniehy made some of the best
speeches ever heard in this Chamber to a House of
thirty members.” Parkes; “I fear the lion,
member will endanger his reputation by speaking
in such terms of speeches he never heard.”
Crick: “I never heard Cicero, but I believe he
was very good. ”
N.S. Wales pays dearly for the services of
“Prime Minister” Parkes. The Sobraon pur-
chase, for instance, is the latest specimen of his
blundering. The ship cost £ll,OOO, and the
fittings will probably cost another £ll,OOO. It is
stated on Imperial authority that but for Parkes’
churlishness the province would have been pre-
sented with the training-ship, Shah, at a saving
of many thousands to the taxpayers. The offer
emanated from the Admiralty; but probably it
reached Parkes when he was busy with his Federa-
tion fad. The Agent-General’s cablegrams ought
to be moved for.
Parkes’ Electoral Bill, if passed as thrown
together, would not give the franchise to the
shearers and the seamen, and would take the
suffrage away from every man who could not read
and write ! The Bill is a disgrace to even Parkes,
and the man who operated the scissors and paste
on his behalf merits a stern reproof. Every Elec-
toral Act in the Australias has been liberally
drawn on to compile another of Parkes’ “great
measures.” The work has been done so badly,
that the closest possible scrutiny will be required
to see that the machinery of the Bill does give a
vote to every citizen, whether literate or illiterate,
stationary or nomadic.
Wilkinson, an obscure M.L.A. (Vic.), died
last week, and the House lost an evening. With-
out being unkind, the democrat must protest
against this sort of thing, and chiefly because it is
a kind of assertion of a corporate spirit in Parlia-
ment, inimical to the view of delegation. Besides,
the system is hypocritical. No one in Parliament
really grieved, as a member of Parliament, over
Wilkinson’s death. They looked at the matter as
calmly as did the surgeon who, scraping with his
knife the heart of his dead friend, said —“ Ah, it
is lime that kills us ! ”
With the advent of the British Parliamen-
tary Labour-party, the Daily Chronicle, the
only London morning paper with really demo-
cratic leanings, has the ball at its foot. From the
Times, Standard, and Post the unprivileged
classes can expect nothing; they can hope for even
less at the hands of that truculent organ of an
execrably venal and creasy bourgeoisie, Levy’s
Daily Telegraph. The Daily News is the
“official Liberal” paper, and the wage-earners
know by now of what the Liberalism of upper
Civil Servants consists. Before the Chronicle,
which has a clean record and is owned by
wealthy men who seem to live in more than a
glimmer of democratic light, now lies the most
glorious chance in the world’s journalism.
Victoria pays its money and takes its
choice between the legislative opinions of two
sapient Scotchmen —M‘Call and Jock Nimmaw.
M'Coll affirms that the admission of Maoriland
into the Federation scheme would weaken the
position from a defence point of view by reason
of the long distance separating that country from
Australia. And anon old Jock rises to protest
that it would be a great advantage for Maoriland
to come into the Federation from a defence point
of view, for she would form a very dangerous
base for the operations of a foreign foe. Mean-
time the question of how any foreign foe can pos-
sibly take possession of Maoriland remains ridi-
culously open.
A writer in the S.M. Herald, in re
woman-suffrage, puts it thus :
In a civilised State, not the man Or the Individual adult,
male or female, is the unit of society. The family is the
Unit of civilised . . . society, and the enfranchise-
ment of families only will give representation to women
without endangering the sanctity and unity of family life.
This should be favoured by women, tor it will be a pre-
mium on marriage. The married are the most trust-
worthy members of society. The single furnish most of
the criminal. . . . The larger the number of married
in the State the better the morals, is most obvious. So
long as young men remained in their parental homes they
could have their share of influence in the political counsels
pf the family. The widowed mother would then vote as
the head of the family, and the sons and daughters, when
they gave hostages to fortune by marrying, would then
have the right to a family franchise. This is the only way
to prevent society from becoming socially disintegrated
by individual political rights being given to all adults.
England is about as funny a Freetracle
country as is New South Wales—or Victoria. It
only applies Frectrade to foodstuffs which it can-
not itself grow, but most consistently doses its
manufactures with Protection. Duties, more or
less heavy, are on—ale and beer, ammunition,
arms, books, butter, candles, cheese, chinaware,
cottons, some descriptions of earthenware, em-
broidery and needlework, ostrich feathers, &c.,
artificial flowers, hair manufactures, iron and
steel goods, opera-glasses, paper, pewter goods,
leather goods, dressed rice, silks, spirits, tallow,
tin, watches, wines, woods (with exceptions), and
woollens. It cannot be argued that these selected
items form any portion of merely revenue-raising
duties; they evince a studied discrimination to
protect manufactures already in vogue in that
alleged Freetrade country.
Gov. Kintore wants the Northern Territory
to be separated from South Australia. This must
come under a Federal Government, but here is
Kintore’s reason ■
. . . How possible it is to look for satisfactory legis-
lation by a Parliament 2000 miles distant, and composed
of gentlemen mainly ignorant of the conditions of
tropical life, and who are swayed by constituencies having
little interest in, or prejudiced against, the methods and
management of tropical colonies.
What about the alternative? A Crown colony
governed not J2OOO miles away but 16,000! That
is what Kintore wants, and presumably it is what
the Imperial Government wants if, as he says,
Kintore was sent across Australia in Imperial
interests !

Said Gladstone to Archbishop Magee : “I
am sorry, my lord, that you disagree with my
method of dealing with Home Rule.” “It is not
your dealing, but your shuffling that I dislike,”
replied Magee.
At an hotel Col. Ingersoll asked if they
had good butter. “Tolerable good butter,” said
the landlord cautiously." “Tolerably good butter,”
said the Colonel; “I would as lief have a tolerably
virtuous wife.”
Sir William Fraser’s recollections of
Disraeli, recently published, have proved beyond
all doubt that Gladstone’s “great” rival was as
hollow a sham as the Primrose League that keeps
his memory yellow.
Riding in a London omnibus, carrying the
head of his victim, Hannah Brown, wrapped up in
brown paper, Greenacre, being told that the fare
was 6d. per head, thereupon remarked that he, in
that case, ought to pay for two.

sumably to be made a K.C.M.G. or something.

Some of the N.S.W. Labour-members are
very restive under the application of a few
parliamentary epithets. One exhibits anger be-
cause he is called a “liar” while another raves
because he is referred to as a “ blackleg.”
There is no truth in the report that
Duncan Gillies is about to marry the widowed
mother of nine children. In fact, there’s no report
to that effect. We have merely imagined the
rumour and contradicted it before it had time to
spread.
The pinnacle of human assurance has been
scaled by a Melbourne ex-policeman, who was
superannuated on a pension of £222 in 1883, and
recently put in a claim for arrears of salary—
nearly £4ooo—earned during his eight years of
retirement.
Good old parson Price, after ministering
at the Independent Church, Launceston (Tas.)
for 55 years, has just passed over at 84 years. He
assumed the pastorate of the church in the year
that Johnny Fawkner left Launceston to annex a
principality on the banks of the Yarra Yarra !

Gladstone held a unique position at the
Jubilee Anniversary of the Colonial Bishoprics
Fund. He was one of the original trustees, and
he stood before the white chokered multitude of
last June 20th as the only living man who had
figured at the opening proo:edings of 1841.
Justice Coleridge, father of the present old Lord
Chief, was one of Gladstone’s colleagues on that
occasion. Archdeacon Hale was another, and the
rest are mouldering in the dust of oblivion,
Spagnoletti: “ I passed two ex-political
V.’s in Collins-street East this afternoon, Yerdon
and Yale, of course at a respectful distance from
each other. Here are two men who, in point of
age, might be Parkes’sons, and yet they evidently
consider themselves played out. Yerdon, retired
from his bank, may be passed regularly at five
every afternoon on his way to dinner at the Mel-
bourne Club, the only event left to him in his day.
Vale is, or was, a remarkable man, the restless
Ballarat bookseller, who, after stormy politics,
went to London and qualified himself as a barris-
ter, though he had heavy family responsibilities.”
Sir William Gordon-Cumming is of the
bluest of blue blood. He is of the blood of Robert
Comyn to whom the Conqueror, in the third year
of his reign, gave the earldom of Northumberland,
and he is of the race of Comyn of Badenoch, men
of stainless honour: one of whom, John Comyn,
claimed the throne of Scotland, and whose two
sons, John and Roger, were both murdered by
Robert Bruce. And the blood of Bruce, as well,
now flows in the veins of Sir William Gumming
through his ancestress, Lucy Gordon, the lineal
descendant of Princess Margaret of Scotland,
through whom he represents the premier Baronets
of that country. And he also represents an
ancient West of England family, Penrose of Pen-
rose, in Cornwall, settled there before the
Conquest.
Colston, the Narbethong murderer, has
left his brain to the doctors, in the hope that it
will enable them to settle the question of his
sanity. Now, a man may have been as mad as a
hatter and yet his brain may show no recognisable
signs of disease. “I have examined over 2000
brains,” we once heard Dr. Neild say, “and yet I
know nothing.” Apropos. Two men, both said
to be shamming madness, were confined in a
southern N.S.W. gaol a few years ago. One, in
an access of rage had chopped down a boy who
had thrown stones at his hut, and was awaiting
execution ; the other had been convicted of horse-
stealing. A medical commission reported both
sane, and the homicide was hanged. The horse-
thief broke gaol and drowned himself in the local
river. A local surgeon opened his skull and found
in his brain a walnut-sized tumour. Yet that
thief may not have been half so mad as the
murderer, in whose brain no disease could be
recognised post-mortem.

One of Disraeli’s friends, Sir William
Eraser, has published a biography of the old,
glaring, garish Hebrew, and the result ought to
knock Primrose Leagueism on the head for good
and all. Disraeli, according to this authority,
doesn’t look like good material to build a Primrose
League upon. He didn’t care for primroses, which
was already well-known, or for flowers of any
kind, or for poetry or scenery, but he had the
Hebrew weakness for glaring at red and blue and
yellow furniture, and his tastes in upholstery were
loud enough to alarm the whole neighborhood,
He was addicted to green velvet pants when he
was young, and shrieking brass jewellery; and
when he was old he had black velvet waistcoats
and howling gold chains instead. He wore big
rings outside his gloves and had a little curl that
hung down about the middle of his forehead. It
looked as if it had been left loose through haste or
negligence, but Disraeli struggled with it for
years'before he got it to hang right. He had the
most successful hair-dye in Europe—in fact, it is
supposed that he made it himself secretly in the
dead of night. He was always oily and more or
, ..I ? ...v,™ ho hnno-rv and
less gentlemanly, except when he was hungiy, ana
then he wasn t gentlemanly. When h
be pathetic he only became cheap ana dismal, ana
he loved immense flunkeys in misfit liveries,
There was a time-payment aspect about all his
wavs and his clothes always seemed unnaturally
J 9 u „««nfnr.Ql Ut dimv nnH he was
new and his boots unnaturaily shiny, and he was
scented too much, and he looked bogus an -y
in all his doings from beginning to end. All tnese
things might have been got over except one—the
green velvet pants which ought to knock out the
Tory idol in one act. An idol in green pants is

Tom Williams, M.L. A. , the shearer who
W AKLoH Hie literary man of the
put down W. E. Abbott, the literary man ui
Pastoralists’ Union, for the Upper Hunter, when
shearing was always in the front rank in uphold-
ing the rights of his guild. He was once working
on a back-country station where the boss (the
well-known Mr. C ) attempted to work off ram-
stags upon the shearers as mutton. The boys
grumbled, but no notice was taken, so the gentle
Thomas was deputed to wait upon the boss and
inform him that unless decent mutton were forth-
coming all hands would leave. The boss stormed
and raved, but finally had to give in on the mutton
question. This was before there was any Union,
and though the price was 20s. per hundred, all
shearers had to agree to be paid off at 15s. if dis-
charged before completion of shearing. Within
one day of the close of shearing Williams and
some others who had spoken their minds pretty
freely on the ram-stag business were “sacked,” and
informed that they would be paid off at 15s. per
100. The other shearers were indignant, but
could do nothing, as the manager had enough
boundary-riders on the station to finish the few
sheep still unshorn, and would have smilingly
paid all hands off at 15s. had they struck. Tom
got his own and mates’ horses up out of the pad-
dock, put their packs on, and then coolly strolled
up to the office with a heavy green-hide riding-
whip in his hand for a settlement. The kind
employer had the accounts made up at 15s. per
100. “Now, Mr. C ,”said Williams, “don’t
let us waste time. It is 25 miles to the nearest
police-station, assistance cannot possibly arrive
from there under five hours, and the shearers will
take great care that no one on the station will
come to your assistance. You’ll either pay me
and my mates in full for the sheep we have shorn,
or I’ll flog you as long as this whip hangs
together.” They got their money in full.
Samuel Vincent Winter has severed his
connection with Melbourne Evening Herald.
He can stand a great deal, can our old boss, and
has served a severe apprenticeship to a variety of
swipy reporters and maudlin compositors. But
then, he has always been able to get off his disgust
when annoyed, by giving forth strong language,
in a broad, gusty, Benbow fashion. This has
saved him from premature apoplexy. Now, how-
ever, even this resource, or palliative, is denied
him. The Herald has passed into the hands of
a syndicate of blue Presbyterians; God-fearing
men, who walk the narrow path of righteousness,
.and know not the comfort of a good, full-bodied,
healthy old blast of blasphemy when the soul is
vexed by any of the miseries incident to the pub-
lication of the evening newspaper. The whole
influence of this salutary change is felt by the old
staff, who have one and all gone off their liquor,
and thinned down perceptibly. Geo. Walstab
has lost much of his tine cavalier boot-and-saddle
swagger, and seems somehow to have been re-
formed into the solemn presentment of a Round-
head or Cropeared trooper. Ellis, the versatile man
of marvellous memory, has ceased to tell his green-
room stories. Jem Middleton has been detected
reading “Pilgrim’s Progress,” while O’Toole,poor
devil, is doing his best to appear as though born
and bred an Ulster Orangeman. Honest Sam
Winter, however, who cannot be all things to all
men, has growled his farewell curse, and left the
Herald to the care of the Elect. McKinley, of
the D.T.—and Punch ! —is to be the manager.
Anybody who remembers the good old, spicy,
stay-out-all-night style of the Herald, cannot
but be edified at the change which has come over
that disreputable old cripple. Sermons and soda-
water have succeeded the gay and giddy dissipa-
tion of the past career of the paper, and as fit and
proper to a visible decay of strength and mental
capacity, and a sure approach to dissolution, the
tone and style are now of the best improved
Christian order, warranted to wear, and well
shrunk. Poor old Herald ! Many have been
its vicissitudes under Kavanagh, Michie, Franklyn,
Levey and Winter; but never in its most de-
praved times did it fall into such humiliation as
now threatens it, at the hands of this Calvinistic
company of godly journalists. Eheu ! Eheu !
Dan O’Connor has just been appointed to
the N.S.W. Legislative Council. This is what he
said (as per Hansard) about the Legislative
Council aforesaid, not so very long ago :
I think it must be patent to every man in the com"
munity that, at no distant date, the Government must
take some action to maintain the honour and dignity of
Parliament by making the Upper House, not a place of
refuge for intellectual incapables, but a home of
patriotism and intellectual power. The members are
appointed at the bidding of ministers of the day. They
owe their existence, as members of the Council, to the
smiles or caprice of ministers, not to any services which
they have rendered, not to transcendent genius w’hich
they have displayed, but because, after a life of sordid
avarice, they desire to have the social distinction which
the position confers on them. Instead of being relegated
to obscurity, they are sent to a place where they have it
ip their power to defy the will of the people. In the
House of Lords are men who have in their veins the blood
of ancestors who have rendered marked services to the
state, men who are distinguished in every walk in life.
Take two or three men away from the Legislative Council
and, in the name of heaven, where can you match them
for a certain quality which 1 would be out of order in
mentioning?
J. H. Jones, Esq., Survey Camp, Toko
block, Taranaki, New Zealand, writes : —“Dear
Sir, —The bottle of Clements Tonic I received
from you gave me great satisfaction and ease.
I suffered from nervous debility and prickly
heat and one bottle of Clements Tonic did me a
power of good, and I intend to always keep it
by me, as it did so much good.” H. Garkell,
Esq., writes on May 20th: “St. Leonards,
Sydney,—Dear sir,—l can with pleasure bear
witness to the great relief I have received from
the use of Clements Tonic and Dr. Fletcher’s
Pills. I have been a great sufferer for 11 years
from liver disease, with at times considerable
enlargement, which caused a swelling in the
side under the ribs and was very painful, the
abdomen, bowels, &c., always felt very tender
on pressxire, with most obstinate constipation,
for which I took Fletcher’s Pills, the first dose
caused a copious evacuation and gave great
relief, and I thought I was all right, but after
a week I was as bad again as before, I again
had recourse to Fletcher’s Pills, but I took
Clements Tonic as well this time and continued
it for a couple of months, after the first dose I
felt better and got rid of all the symptoms I
used to have, as flushings of heat and cold,
splitting headaches, pains in the side and small
of the back, extreme lassitude, and the general
feeling of ‘ all right to-day, seedy to-morrow,’
all these symptoms with many others I used to
have, but now, thanks to Clements Tonic, I am
quite cured. I can get up in a morning re-
freshed by the night’s rest, and can eat a good
breakfast, whereas before taking the medicine
I scarcely ever could cat anything, and when I
did, it nearly always made me vomit, but now I
am quite well and have been so for 5 months.
I have no cause to fear a relapse. I don’t mind
your publishing my ease if you add nothing to
it, as it may bring relief to similar sufferers.”
Thomas Oliver, Esq., proprietor Lillydale
Express, Vic., writes under date Nov. 10th,
’9O :—Dear Sir, —About two months ago Mrs.
Oliver went to Sydney as weak and helpless as
a child, notwithstanding the doctors had tried
to baffle her complaint. Her friends in Sydney
(with whom she is at present) persuaded her to
take Clements Tonic, and from reports the
results are almost magical, instead of only
being able to walk 100 yards she can now walk
half a mile without the least trouble. She
hopes to return shortly, she says, ‘a new
creature’ thanks to Clements Tonic,” Such
grateful acknowledgments as these must carry
conviction to the most sceptical mind; the
great pity is that owing to the wonderful
efficacy of this remedy, unscrupulous traders
offer substitutes in place of Clements Tonic
when this article is demanded. Patients must
remember that a man who will sell an inferior
article for the sake of extra profit is a villain
and a rogue, for he not only robs you of your
money, but trifles with your health, for whilst
you fancy you are secure, the disease is making
headway all the time. Beware of these
swindlers and expose them to your neighbours,
and buy no Tonic that does not bear the
signature of F. Moore Clements in blue ink on
wrappers and labels, and the address, New-
town, N.S.W.

A certain learned professor was travelling
towards Sydney University in a crowded tram.
Suddenly a man left the corner opposite to
him, exposing the professor’s umbrella to the
vulture-like eye of a lady who sat at the other
end of the compartment. Arriving at her point
of exit, she said to the professor, “Will you kindly
hand me down my umbrella?” “Madam,” said
he, emphatically, “ this is my umbrella! ”
“Pshaw!” replied the dame, clearly “on the
make,” “ you claim the umbrella because you
think it belongs to the gentleman that just got
out.” “Pardon me, madam,” politely and neatly
said the professor, “ but that statement describes
your position, not mine. ” The passengers grinned
widely, and the lady got out awkwardly without
the parapluie.
* * *
A nude woman, the other day, walked
right down the main street of Richmond (Melb.),
ducking herself in the public-house horse-troughs
as she went along —decidedly an improper way to
water one’s whisky. The Sydney papers, in
printing a telegram about the matter, label it
“ A Maniac’s Freak.” Why necessarily a maniac ?
For all they know, the woman is as sane as James
Service, or Bruce Smith, or Sam. Griffith, and
does this kind of thing out of pure cussedness.
The papers would not admit that Johnston, who
did a still more insane act, was mad ; nor do they
admit that there is any chance of Colston being
insane. “ But the woman had no motive,”people
may say. Nonsense ! She may fancy that she
has no end of a figure. Maniac, indeed !
* # *
The recent report as to £lO,OOO having been
offered Sir Henry Parkes for a history of Aus-
tralia during the last 50 years, was, of course, mere
nonsense on the face of it. Who would think of
going for a history to a politician who fancied
himself the chief actor in the era described ? Then
again, the price would be colossal —even Brigand
Stanley, advertised in all languages, only got 50
per cent, more for his fine imaginative efforts.
The N.S.W. Premier’s autobiography, if skilfully
and carefully done, would be of enormous Aus-
tralian and general interest; and last year Sir
Henry offered to supply something of the kind to
a Sydney man for £lOOO and half-profits, but
apparently no strong local publisher could be
found to take it up on those terms.
# # *
Mr. Charles Jewel Oliver was born under
a lucky star. For having his services as a N.S.W.
Under-Secretary “dispensed with” at his own
request, he has been awarded a pension of £360 a
year, while he is drawing £l5OO a year as a super-
numerary Railway Commissioner. Is it not time
this farce were denounced in Parliament, and the
Superannuation Fund conserved for the relief of
old State servants? The Civil Service Board
must be composed of sorry civil servants, to allow
themselves to be “ used ” in this illegal fashiou.

Spicy ads. from a Sydney paper :
Wanted by widow lady, loan £l5, pay back instalments.
Wanted a kind gent, to befriend a respectable lady in
distress.
Would kind gentleman lend young widow in trouble few
pounds at once ?
Would elderly gentleman lend a widow in difficulties a
few pounds?
Would elderly gentleman of means oblige widow lady
with £5 privately ?
Will some kind gentleman lend a widow £5, paid back
weekly ?
Will gentleman assist a dressmaker in difficulties with a
few pounds?

Lord Jersey has been telling some official
fibs. Outside the boundaries of N.S.W. he signed
a batch of gubernatorial messages as at “ Govern-
ment House, Sydney.” The Constitution enacts
that a Governor shall only exercise the functions
of his office within the sphere of his jurisdiction ;
and as Lord Jersey did not appoint a deputy to
act in his absence, the point is whether the
messages are valid, and, if not, whether all the
Government measures are not improperly intro-
duced, including the Electoral Bill.

* * *
Lord and Lady Jersey have been con-
ducted through the underground workings of the
great Broken-hill Proprietary silver-mine. Will
someone now please conduct them through all the
underground workings of the wild-cat companes ?
They would be edified.
People are getting pretty sick of Sydney news-
paper (daily and weekly) mining advice. The
Bulletin presumes that the exhortations to “buy
so-and-so and so-and-so if yon have to pawn your
coat,” tendered every week by one classical pub-
lication to the world at large, benefit somebody.
They certainly don’t benefit that credulous por-
tion of the public which takes the tip; to con-
sistently follow, on a reasonably large scale, that
scribe’s advice would have broken even a
Jemmy Tyson. It makes one laugh to see shares
cracked up at 2s. lOd. and watch the faces of those
responsible for the crack-up as, two days later,
said shares are being unavailingly rushed round
in 5000 parcels at a modest “ bob.”
* * #
Charley Kingston, Q.C., of Adelaide,
boldly states that a knowledge of Latin is of
little or no use to a lawyer. He also holds that
doctors’ prescriptions should be written in Eng-
lish. What would the chemists do in the latter
event ? How could they have the gall to charge
3s. 6d. for threepen’orth of sal-volatile, syrup of
orange, and water, coloured with solution of burnt
sugar? Or Is. 6d. for a ha’porth of sulphate of
zinc, 10 drops of opium wine, and water as an eye-
lotion? But let the mixture read: —“ R. —Sp.
Ammon. Aromat. (and then something like three
or four wooden soldiers outside a sentry-box);
Syr. Aurant. (more hieroglyphics); Sacch. Ust.
(more again); Aq. @ (and the final lot);” with a
mystery instruction —“Ft. mist; cap. coch. mag.
T ter in die ;” or again, let the lotion read —“R. —
Zinci Sulph. (soldiers) ; Yin. Opii (soldiers) ; Aq.
@ (soldiei’s) ; Ft. lotic, u.t.d.” —why, the whole is
a cabalistic charm, and the art of charging top-
prices is established on a firm basis.
# * *
The N.S.W. Premier discovers that the
Australian Agents-General should be changed
every three or four years. Is he ready to sacrifice
his friend of half-a-century as another “sop” to
the new power? He further thinks that the A.-G.-
ship should be a non-political office filled by a
succession of colonists “ of independent means and
who would not take it for the sake of the salary”—
another way of saying that the representation of
Australia at the world’s hub should be the special
perquisite of a class instead of going to the ablest
man available out of all classes. By the way, if
Sir Saul Samuel was studiously discourteous to
Mr. Fitz Gerald, the N.S.W. Labour delegate, in
London, he must surely either have had official
instructions to be uncivil, or Fitz. must have run
up against him when he had the gout. Old Sir
Saul has, up to date, been found by travelling
Australians of all grades to be an exceedingly kind
and considerate man.

A Privy Council appeal, in a case decided
by N.S.W. Full Court in ’B7, has been decided in
July of the year of our Lord 1891. The respon-
dent has meanwhile died, and the sum at stake
has all been swallowed up in costs. Truly, the
Australians are a long-suffering and law-abiding
people. # # #
The Anglican Synod, held in Sydney last
week, was opened with an attendance of 59 clergy -
men and 92 laymen, but the preliminary devotional
service in the Cathedral, intended to invoke the
Holy Spirit to preside over the Synod, was
attended by only 12 clergymen and two laymen.
The vulgar reading of this is that the Synod now
thinks that it can do very well without the
patronage of the Almighty.
* * *
It is long since we reprinted any hunks of
wisdom from the pen of that great master of
vigorous English, Ambrose Bierce. Read :
The writer who calls a social entertainment a “function”
is apparently in no peril of starvation: he has steady
employment on all the newspapers. There is comfort,
though, in the bright hope that he may hang, and that
great ravens may come from the four quarters of the sky
to fight for his eyes ; that the green and rancid fat may
drip from the toes of him, begetting the abundant maggot
beneath; that his fragrance may impest ten provinces
and unpeople the most ancient city in all the world !
These be cheery possibilities whereon the Christian soul
joys to dwell, remembering his great unworth. For God
hath said He will not hold him guiltless who mocks not
when evil comes upon the fool, nor prosper these who
mourn him. Wherefore, in forehanded fulfillment of the
Law let us gibe and gird and make our mouths marvel-
lous, even to-day, lest when the time of his punishment
be at hand we be worms’-meat ourselves.
* * *
Once more the Bierce :
There never was a prince nor a king who was a gentle-
man ; a princess nor a queen who was a lady. You may
search authentic history from alpha to omega and not
one instance can you find. Of those concerning whom
enough is known to justify an estimate of character all
have been vulgarians. In the nature of things it must
be so. The first requisite in the character of a gentleman
is his sense of equality with all other gentlemen : the
least assumption of superiority is fatal to the character.
The entire education of a prince, everything in his en-
vironment, both before and after he becomes a king if he
is that kind of royal pollywog, tends to keep him in
mind of his superiority to all about him. He comes to
believe in it, as a thing intrinsic and essential. He
permits himself to be addressed by titles signifying an
inherent and substantial difference between him and his
fellow mortals because he believes that such is the fact.
His irresponsibility for infractions of social and moral law,
the servility of all with whom he comes in contact, make
him with absolute certainty just what I have said—a
blackguard and a vulgarian. To a less degree the same is
true of “ the nobility and aristocracy.” It is nearly im-
possible that they should be gentlemen and ladies in that
high sense in which those use the words wlm use them
least. It is antecedently improbable and in point of fact
obviously untrue that “ the upper circles ” in any country
but a republic which is a republic in nature as well as in
name (as ours is not) are distinguished for moral worth.
They are “ a bad lot” the world over, and those of Eng-
land are probably not the worst despite our reverent
genuflexions at their “shrines.”

The absurd judgment given in favour of

seduction, in an English court, was based on

rather reminds one of the cruel Sydney experiences
of Mr. Myles Mcßae.

* * #
It isn’t good form, in the very best British
circles, to cross a street diagonally. You may cut
off a considerable angle and save time, but the
proceeding lacks tone, and isn’t first-class. If a
person moving in the highest society wants to get
to the other side of the thoroughfare, she should
go right on till she gets opposite the place she is
aiming at—then stop suddenly as if she had
catalepsy or had seen a spectre —next describe a
quarter-circle, fetching out somebody’s eye with
her umbrella as she does it, and go right across in
a bee-line. When she gets to the middle of the
opposite pavement she makes another sharp turn,
and impales a fresh infant with her sunshade, and
goes on her way serenely. It is a sure sign of a
neglected education if she looks surprised when
she impales the infant. A person of the very high-
est culture will stop and talk in mangled French
to a passing acquaintance, with a child in a par-
tially disembowelled state entangled on the end of
her gingham, and never even change countenance.

The just-introduced N.S.W. Criminal Law
Amendment Bill places the dishonest civil servant
of the future on the same footing as the common
or garden rogue. In the case of the latter it is
and has been sufficient to_ prove a general em-
bezzlement ; in that of a civil servant each em-
bezzled item must be made the subject of a
separate indictment. Henceforth gentlemen in
the service who “stumble,” will have to plead to
a lump sum, “ Guilty or not guilty, av ye plaze.”

Condensed remark from an Ipswich (Q.)
paper:—
Queensland has been smitten in the face with the
Naval Defence Bill, and to-day she lies prostrate, her
power of self-government rent in twain, the chief part
torn from her, and the remnant lying in her helpless
hands bleeding to death.
Just so. Also in her dreams she sees the bright
lexicon of youth bounding through the primeval
forests, and the vanished grace of a day that is
gone has been butchered to make a Roman holi-
day, and Freedom has croaked owing to tumultu-
ous horror having got there with both feet.
* * *
As a rule, the woman whose dress is cut
lowest among all the crowd in the circle is the
immense, corpulent female of 47, whose arms look
like two red sacks of flour, while the great desert
of her shiny flesh expands so far that you feel
tired before you have walked twice round the bare
part of her. And the woman who runs her very
close in the matter of scarcity is the scrawny indi-
vidual of 17, whose bones jut out like crags and
pinnacles and old yellow knobs in a great waste
land of skin. When you find a plump and fresh-
looking maiden among the crowd it is three
chances to one that she is so disguised in bits of
lace, muslin, and other small mysteries that she is
about as invisible as if she had no evening dress
at all. Heaven knows why this is—we don’t.
The bare and mountainous old female, or her
famine-stricken sister, might be able to explain it,
but we have never had the pluck to ask either of
them.

One of the most “ swagger ” house-boats
at Henley this year is hired by Mount Morgan
Knox D’Arcy. The decorations cost a small
fortune, and fairly give Colonel North the knocks.
The gilded D’Arcy is a most hospitable fellow,
and keeps open table on board. Needless to say
that Bananalanders turn up religiously in scores
when the gong sounds.

There is much Melbourne discussion over
Miss Pickett’s death at Colombo. Persuaded by
Madame Blavatsky’s particular friend, Col.
Olcott, she went there, from Melbourne, to take
charge of the Buddhist Girls’ School, but it
appears that, being short-sighted, she fell in a
well, and was drowned. On the other hand,
suicide is alleged.

The leading Melbourne caterer was
engaged, not long ago, to provide for the Young
Women’s Christian Association, at the Town
Hall, but they found out he was an alcohol-
merchant, and deputationised to argue and pray
him out of it. He assured them that he only
attended to their little affair as a matter
of benevolence, and nothing ever paid him
where alcohol was not involved. This was indeed
a neat stroke. Pious people who like not beer
have enormous appetites.

“SHE FOLLOWED HIM HOME.”
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
“About three weeks ago we chronicled the
death of Mr. . It is now our sad duty to
announce the demise of his widow who has abso-
lutely died of grief at her loss. ’’—[Daily Paper.)
Her soul through magnificent space
Has passed up beyond the deep blue
Of the sky, and the light on her face
Is lovely, and radiant and new ;
With never a sign or a trace
Of the sorrow and pain that she knew.
From the folds of a fierce sudden woe
She has fled where the days are all bleat—
Where a balm for her grief she will know
In the arms of (he one she loved best.
And the parting thatr'saddencd her so
Is atoned for with infinite rest.
In a far-distant season long dead
And buried away in the past —
She had sworn to be true, and she said—
“ Until death, Oh, my darling, 1 cast
My life in with yours”—his has fled ;
But she followed him home at the last.
Brisbane, 2nd Aug., 1891.
H. E. P. M‘DONALD.
MORE TRIOLETS.
This Triolet metre is rot.
That girl at the pub. is a “ snorter ; ”
She calls me a “ naughty young sot ”
(This Triolet metre is rot)
Because I can lower a lot
Of glasses of whisky and water—
(This Triolet metre is rot)
That girl at the pub. is a “ snorter !”
I always address her as “Floss,”
Although her right name is Elizer.
Unless she looks haughty and cross
I always address her as “ Floss” —
(For the reason I’m quite at a loss,
And don’t suppose you are much wiser)—
1 always address her as “ Floss,”
Although her right name is Elizer.
I doat on that girl at the pub.,
(Especially when I am squiffy),
And could she make puddings, or scrub
(I doat on that girl at the pub.),
She’d not have to hunt for a hub.—
Bedad ! I’d propose in a jiffy.
I doat on that girl at the pub.
{Especially when I am squiffy.)

A London paper states that the Prince of
Wales has become “markedly aged and careworn ”
since the baccarat scandal got wind, and his hair
(which chiefly runs to beard, by the way) is
“ visibly grizzled.” The melancholy lute, if you
please; —
Since the Hotel Metropole, Sydney, was
licensed to sell liquors, it pays a dividend of 8 per
cent. As a coffee-palace, it was a spec, out of
which its owners could only expect to draw divi-
dends in Heaven. By the way, teetotaldom
will be painfully interested to learn that at the
big Australia Hotel, Sydney, more than 150
gallons of over-proof whisky are being consumed
weekly.
* * *
Mrs. Newmann, of Logan Reserve (Q.),
some ten years ago began her family with triplets
—three boys—and was granted the “Queen’s
bounty.” Since then she has been adding to the
Bananaland population “ by two and by three.”
On 24th June, the last trio arrived—two boys and
one girl. Total to date : 18, of whom 15 are
living.
* # *
The M‘Meckan (Melb.) will-case cost over
£7500. The barristers absorbed £2280, the solici-
tors (who paid witnesses, &c.) took double as
much. The jury got £2B 12s. each, or £343 4s. in
all. The trial lasted 17 days. Purves, Q.C.,
drew 625 guineas ; Wood and Madden, 490
guineas; Topp and Gold ham, 355 guineas.
• * »
Mem. for lady witnesses. Purves, Q.C.,
said that Miss Mackie need not have been un-
truthful about her age, as she was not bound to
have answered the question. Higinbotham, C. J.,
did not object to this statement, so it may pass as
good law. But how would this work in a
marriage-promise case in which the bride had
understated her age ?

“A well-educated sense of humour will
save a woman when religion, training and home
influences fail,” says Rudyard Kipling. Quite
right, but—as the cookery-books don’t say : “First
catch your humorous woman.” If women had
any humour a lot of things would happen. For
instance, the clergy would have to chop wood for
a living.

The gallant Queensland cavalry “ volun-
teers” are now doing some underground engineer-
ing with a view to getting paid ; and the local
military officers have had a board appointed to
examine their claims to extra remuneration on
account of special strike services. So these
warriors are not disposed to live on votes of
thanks. They recall in a roundabout way the
more honest Soudan contingenteer who when
asked, “What are you going to fight for?” replied,
“ Five bob a day and tucker.”
m • *
Concerning the influence of climate on
manners. In the rural Maoriland districts up
north the inscription generally reads : “ You are
requested to close this gate,” In the middle pro-
vinces, where man is a shade less civilised, it
appears in a shorter form: “Please close this
gate. ” Down among the savages of Otago and
Southland, the brief paragraph on the top bar is
simply: “Shut this gate.” You can’t get any
further south than that.

Licensing-Inspector Attewell successfully
opposed the granting of a publican’s license last
week in Sydney, on the ground that the applicant
was a woman, and that the house she sought to
license could not be managed by a woman. Thus
is the question of the equality of the sexes settled,
once for all, in a bold, policeman-like manner.

North Shore (Sydney) has a private gas
Co., or rather a private gas Co. “has” North
Shore (Sydney). The Company wants £90,000 for
its property. The Council can do it for under
£60,000 and mean to do it. Hereupon the
Company whines and says that widows and
orphans have shares in the Company which will
be ruined by Council competition. The Council
rejoins that widows and orphans are being ruined
by gas-bills. The Council is going ahead, anyhow.
Dear Bulletin, —In yesterday’s Argus there is
a sub-leader re loss of ships, which remarks that when
there were few or no lighthouses or tugs, wrecks were
uncommon, but that now they are many. The
master, says the Argus, “ must have degenerated.” Of
course there are more wrecks now, for in days gone by
the shipmaster—who must degenerate under existing
conditions—was fairly paid and treated, and was allowed
to manage the ship both as regards sailing and loading
and manning ; but how different now! Take the master
of an ordinary large sailing-ship. His wages are often
£l2 per calendar month, or Bs. per day (little better than
labourer’s wages), and he is put in charge of a ship per-
haps over £20,000 in value, with perhaps £50,000 worth of
cargo on board and the lives thrown in. To obtain this
high position he has to pass three examinations and the
best part of a life at sea. He has little or no voice in the
loading or manning of his ship, and is liable to be dis-
missed at any port at any part of the world at any inomen'.
The next person in command is the chief office r
whose wages range about four shillings per day ; then
comes the second-officer, at often 2s. Bd. per day. Take
a large mail-steamer worth perhaps half a million pounds,
carrying hundreds of lives and thousands of pounds of
cargo, where, should anything happen to her master, the
chief-officer takes charge. This gentleman, you will find,
this gentleman often starts at 6s. Bd. per day, always has
to dress well, wear expensive livery, mix with people of
means, and yet it takes him 10 to 15 years to obtain this
position. I have not said anything of the hardships, loss
of home comforts and recreation, incidental to the life of
a seaman, nor to the fearful risks so often undertaken to
make fast passages, the alternative to which is the
“sack.” I am now referring to shipping companies
generally. To sum up, the shipmaster has degenerated
through bad pay, overwork, and bad treatment, and the
losses are swelled by overloading and undermanning. As
I dare not here write ray name, I will simply subscribe
myself— Constant Reader, Melbourne, July 19, ’9l.

We are told that when the “scab”
shearers from Sydney reached a black-block
(N.S.W.) station the other day, eight of them took
all day to shear 96 sheep.
In answer to a remark in the organ of a
Bourke M.P., The Bulletin has to say that it
has seen through, and has not sympathised with,
the sinister efforts made to work up a row be-
tween the police and the unionist shearers. The
political crowd who have been egging the shearers
on to violence, despite the prospect of an amicable,
even if but temporary, arrangement, are a set
whose word no one will take, and whose most
sacred promise is not worth a copper token. They
would sneak into wombat holes in the event of a
fight, and, if there were no longer any prospect of
making capital out of the trouble; would not
put their hands into their pockets to save insur-
gent bushworkers from being hanged as high as
Hainan. The road to reform lies, not through
physical violence, while entrenched monopoly has
a clear and overwhelming advantage, but through
the Legislature ; and any politician who, with his
tongue in his cheek, “kids on” the unarmed!
shearer to threaten violence against the law, with
its gun in its hand, is the bitterest enemy of those
he gammons to serve. The fighting plan has been
tried and has failed. The Parliamentary plan is
now being tried and threatens to succeed —much
to the chagrin of certain mangy politicians whose
game it is to prove that the workman, voting-
ticket in hand, cannot be his own political
saviour.
The Australian Workman upholds the
principle of “ One-Man-One-Billet,” and contro-
verts the Bartonian assertion that —
If members have to confine themselves solely to politics
the country will lose the services of many men of in-
tellect and experience, who cannot give (.sic) those services
it doing so deprived them of attendance on their business.
“ The sooner we lose the service of those business
men the better,” says The Workman —
The legislation of this country, the making of good
laws and the repealing of bad ones, the solution of the
problems which are agitating the minds of all white
people, demand that a politician shall give up the whole
of his time to serving the interests of his country. If a
man is not prepared to do this then he has no right to be
a member of Parliament; and be his intellect what it
may, we have plenty of intellectual and experienced men
who are prepared to give the whole of their time and brain
power to the interests of their country. “One-man-one-
billet” is our motto.
This sounds like the utterance of a man who, in
the first flush of getting £3OO a year for his Par-
liamentary services—twice as much, perhaps, as
he ever earned before in his life —or smarting
under defeat at the hands of men who could hope-
fully’ aspire to more than £3OO a year, seeks to
make all people conform to his own standard.
Yon could not get the very best, or even the most
mediocre, men in the community to confine them-
selves absolutely to politics for the present rate of
Australian parliamentary pay, and as most seats
are uncertain at the best of times, the man who
relied solely on them as a means of livelihood
would show himself to be an improvident fool.
Had “ One-man-one-billet ” been the rule, scores
of the best men who have sat in Parliament would
have been excluded, and were it the rule now
the Houses would be largely' composed of mere
shiftless, needy seekers after parliamentary screw,
and puffy people “of independent means.” In
N.S. Wales, for instance, G. H. Reid and Barton
would be out of Parliament for a start ; so would
Bruce Smith and M‘Millan— The Bulletin, while
working for the ascendancy of Labour, hopes to
see the best men from all sides in the Legisla-
ture — and sj would not only every other man
who could not live on £3OO a year, but everyone
who, in the open market, could earn more. If
a member does get £3OO a year he is expected to
give away half of it, just as the Minister is
expected to give away £6OO or £7OO of his £l5OO.
The object of granting pay to members of Parlia-
ment is not necessarily to shut rich men out —
that is, men who are rich because they are
highly paid for duties other than political, but
to let poor men in. It is in the direction of
equalising opportunities. A rich man may be a
good democrat. Sir George Grey, for example,
is a rich man. The man who can best afford to
fulfill the one-man-one-billet condition is the pro-
perty-owner, of necessity utterly out of sympathy
with labour legislation.
FROM GAY TO GRAY.
The sun it shineth brightly
On a petted royal youth
When his step is gay and sprightly,
And he hasn’t lost a tooth;
Life once was all to-morrow
For the giddy coming K.—
Now, he wears a look of sorrow.
And his hair’s gone gray.
'Twas nice to be a Bertie
In hig podgy golden prime }
Till long past five-and-thirty
He enjoyed a jolly time;
But* the passion flower withers
As the summer fades away—
O, alas ! for royal brithers
When their hair turns gray.
Lovely woman’s sweetest glances
Greet his Highness as of yore,
But the taste for “ passing fancies ’*
He has lost for evermore ;
The life that was so pleasant
Seems a thing of yesterday,
’Twere as well to be a peasant
Now his hair’s gone gray.
Such a spectacle pathetic
Is not very often seen ;
The effect is quite magnetic
On the Sydney Bullytebn ;
Though, perhaps, we have been ready
Rather cruel things to say,
We’ll be kind to Albert Eddy
Now his hair’s gone gray.
“Radam’s Microbe Killer.”
22nd January, 189 L
60 and 62 Elizabeth-street,
Melbourne.
Radam’s Microbe Killer Co.
Gentlemen, —Some time since you kindly gave
to an applicant of the Melbourne Jewish Philan-
thropic Society two jars of your “ Microbe
Killer.” The unfortunate man was in a bad state
from consumption, and very little hopes were
entertained for his ultimate recovery. After
taking five jars of your “Microbe Killer ” he found
great relief, and, continuing with same, is now all
but thoroughly recovered and able to earn a living
for his wife and family. You are at perfect liberty
to make what use you think fit of this testimonial,
and I can vouch for its correctness in every
particular.—Yours, &c.,
S. DAVIES
(of Lazarus, Rosenfeld, and Benjamin),
Treas. Melbourne Jewish Philanthropic Society.

Yes, you are a Civil servant and are subject to the pro-
visions of the Civil Service .Act which provides for your
pay being docked on account of the superannuation fund.
“W.J.C.”: Rather rough. We don’t go out of our
way to deal with religious questions....“ Artaxerxes” :
Nothing under Heaven is new- not even your foolish
letter. Tub Bulletin is not absolutely original it is
only as original as it can be. To show all you know about
the matter it is only necessary to state that the alleged
joke in question was not stolen from the Family Herald
—which steals nearly all its paragraphs—but probably
from the source whence the F.H. stole it..,. “Blue
Gum”: Will come to them shortly. Both type-written...
“Rationalist”: The kingly superstition is largely a con-
sequence of the theological one, as you say.... “ Golden
Fleece” : All said before.... “Minnamurra ” : Your verses
lack oongruity... “B.B.”: Too gushy and too hack-
neyed “Republican Australian”: We admire your
sentiments, but pooh-pooh your spelling, anathematise
your handwriting, scoff at your rhymes, and execrate
your rhythm....“ Alexandra”: No room for purely
parochial politics “ Kumanave Adrink”: The letter
of “Paterfamilias” has aroused considerable controversy
—it has been taken too seriously. Its purport was appa-
rently to show one of the tendencies towards inequality
in prevailing conditions, which can only be removed by
their alteration....“ Navigator”: Not in our way ...
“ A.M.L. ” : The Bulletin no more believes in Parkesnow
than it ever did. It regards his political existence as
utterly noxious and absolutely degrading to the public
life of Australia. Trying to use as a tool an insincere old
schemer, who has so far used all other men as tools, is, we
admit, an experiment, but nevertheless one worth trying,
(2) as “Mister Gladstone” is just now advocating One-
Man-One-Vote Parkes may push it through on the “me
ar.d Mister Gladstone” principle... “ Yarrawonga ” :
The principle of betterment is undoubtedly a righteous
one. Corollary: If a specific agreement respecting
reservation for railway construction appears in the title,
which moans that the land was only allowed to be taken
over subject to a defined agreement, the whole ground of
complaint is covered. Let the matter be fought out in
the law courts and see where you will be. It may be
hard on the purchaser from the original holder ; but he
only purchases “within” the title, and is supposed to
have horse-sense enough to know it and examine the said
title.... “W. Fitz ” ; Thanks, but next time try and boil
it down “If we have your story you haven’t written
your name on the face thereof. Name it, please? ...
“ Carbine ” (Adelaide): Geo. A. is certainly right. If you
like you can have a (properly-accredited) representative
to see it worked off “Win. H. K.”; At variance with
Bulletin ideas... “J. Court”: W.P.8.,. “T.G.S.”; Thanks ..

writing on Socialism should drop generalities and get to

“Hans Machputy”: Too lengthyA. M'L.”:

SPORTING NOTIONS.
The six-foot-two figure of the Earl of West-
moreland has disappeared from the English turf and
sought seclusion in the family vault. His ludship went
"broke” at an early period of his racing career, but the
“books ” he owed money to must have died long since,
leaving hint to settle down amidst an odour of sanctity as
a more or less perpetual steward of the Jockey Club. The
“home ” sporting papers will, no doubt, relate many in-
teresting reminiscences of this colossal identity, who
would have made a splendid earl “on the stoush ” a
couple of centuries back. As things went this man of
long descent was simply so much bone and sinew wasted. "

* * *
Felix Tanner, the fasting gentleman,, has started
business in Adelaide. This time he is fasting in an
abandoned’ boot-shop, which arrangement has an unpro-
misi v r look about it, somehow. Probably the fasting
industry is about played out. The Bulletin suggests, in
order to revive this decaying industry, that there should
be two fasting men, and that they should fast 40 weeks,
and then fight each other to a finish for £lOOO and a
funeral. If this wouldn’t infuse some vigour into the
proceedings, nothing would.
# * *
One Cussen, aged 19 years, 6ft. 4in. in height,
the other day at Loneragan’s, Mudgee (N.S.W.), carried
(so says a local paper) a 2001 b.-bag of flour under each arm
for 20 yards. Now, many years ago there was in the Vic-
torian police a trooper named Forbes, who weighed under
list., and yet could ascend a 14-step set of stairs with
2001 b. of flour under each arm.

London Referee maintains that the Kilrain
w ho fought Slavin is the wreck of the man who really
knocked out Smith. “If an evil spirit,” says the Ref.,
■“might wreak his wicked will in sending a big, powerful,
plucky, well-built, loose-limbed, very decent man indeed
to physical ruin, such a demon could not run an athlete
faster on the downward road to perdition than Kilrain
raced himself after he achieved in England celebrity.”
Nevertheless, the American sporting reporters—not that
they are infallible authorities-desoribed Jake’s condition
as perfect when the Australian gave him worst. Con-
dition counts for nothing without constitution.

ensuring first-class workmanship at moderate fees.
