The marmalade ladled out to Premier
Park es by the Pall Mall Gazette is attributed
by the London correspondent of the S. A.
Advertiser to the fact that the Pall Mall is
edited by a brother-in-law and regular corres-
pondent of B. R. Wise.
The other day Judge Docker (N.S.W.)
sentenced two men found guilty of violently
assaulting a fellow prisoner in gaol—one to ten
years’ additional penal servitude and the other to
seven years additional, each to have three hog-
gings of 25 lashes. A day or two afterwards, on
a pretext too flimsy to be discussed under the
circumstances, the Judge called up the prisoners
again and remitted the floggings. We hope that
the Judge was ashamed of himself—or of the Law.
We presume that any civilized being would be
ashamed of a law so barbarous and illogical as to
add flaying alive to ten years “extra” penal
servitude.
Mr. and Mrs. Eather, of Windsor (N.S.W.),
have just got out of gaol. Twelve months ago
someone brought a slander-action against Mrs.
Eather, in which her husband was “joined.” A
verdict was given for the plaintiff, and the unfor-
tunate pair, being moneyless, had, under the
iniquitous N.S.W. Defamation Act, to go to gaol,
where they have since languished apart from their
young children, who have been the recipients
of charity. Imprisonment for debt has therefore
not been abolished in N.S.W. And allowing, for
the sake of argument, that Mrs. Eather deserved
12 months’ gaol for her slander, is it not altogether
against the general tendency of the marriage laws
that her husband should be made thus responsible
for her act ? How would this kind of thing be
regarded in any semi-civilised country—say in
England ?

Secured Deposits. —ln view of the promised
legislation to regulate the business of those finan-
cial institutions which receive money on deposit
from the public, we are pleased to notice that a
step in the right direction has been taken by the
Universal Land and Deposit Bank, Limited, who,
in a little pamphlet entitled “Mutual Banking,”
have called attention to a few points of practical
interest to all bank depositors. The main idea of
the above company appears to be that the
depositors, not the shareholders, should be the
first to receive the benefit of the profits made by
the bank, whilst, by the issue of debentures, they
(the depositors) obtain lien over all the
assets of the concern ana are in possession of a
collateral security equal to the sum deposited,
which, by the way, bears interest. The scheme
is entirely novel, and we think the originators
deserve the support of the public in their efforts
to improve the position of the depositor. We
commend the above-mentioned pamphlet to the
attention of our readers who contemplate invest-
ing their money in this manner. It is forwarded
post free to any address by the bank, whose office
is at 301 Pitt-street, Sydney.*
"KEEP NO FOR REFERENCE.
The following suggestions aie not designed to
induce the public to attempt the duties of the
regular surgeon, but merely to place the reader in
possession of a means of treatment of the minor
accidents occurring daily in the household. No
family can be assured against accidents; the
broken bone, the dislocated joint, the severed
artery, these are misfortunes that may possibly
happen to anyone ; and we scarcely need to add
that they demand the instant attention of the
most skilful surgeon within reach. But by house-
hold accidents we mean those which are constantly
occurring every day, in nearly every family, and
which, while not always dangerous in themselves,
are exceedingly annoying. Burns, bruises, cuts,
scalds, sprains, &c., are principal among these
troublesome and annoying occurrences, and
demand immediate treatment with the best means
at band. In the kitchen, the dining-hall, the
nursery, and the sitting-room they are liable to
happen, and instead of fear and alarm at the sight
of the cut or smashed finger, or burned or bruised
arm, or scalded surface, a cool and quiet manner
should be assumed, and after washing away the
blood (if required), the injured parts should be
dressed with that most valuable remedy—St.
Jacobs Oil. Its especial worth for the injuries
named, and others of similar nature, is not based
upon mere claim and assertion ; but its constant
use for a long term of years has given it a
reputation which is always increasing. Its sur-
prisingly quick relief, its cleansing properties, it s
tendency to quickly remove all inflammation, and
its wonderful efficacy in the above, as well as in
all muscular and other pains, such as rheumalis n,
neuralgia, toothache, headache, stiffness of the
limbs, joints, &c., these qualities render St. Jacobs
oil pre-eminently the best external remedy before
the people, which claim is fully substantiated by
the class of strong testimony voluntarily accorded
it by representatives of every class and station in
life.
ASSAULTS ON WOMEN.
At present it is only a suspicion that the police
have, but something may be expected to come
out of it. Something usually does, even if it is
only a proof that they were entirely in the wrong,
and there was no ground tor suspicion anyhow.
But they may be in luck this time, and everybody
hopes they are, because these assaults on females
in lonely places are becoming scandalously
frequent, and it is more than likely that they
never will be quite stopped until the male relatives
of the victims turn out for the purpose, and then
somebody will get shot, or badly bruised, and the
police will get there in time to recognise the
remains of the man they suspected all along, and
followed for miles, and saw him do all torts of
things except the one particular thing they wanted
him to do, and which thing he would not do,
because he knew them, and stopped to have a
smoke and a yarn with them, and found out as
much about their business as they, knew them-
selves ; and then, like a mean skunk, went off in
another direction and did it again somewhere
else. And if he should some night be shot in the
act so much the better for all the survivors,
because if bo is only taken alive some humanitarian
crank, who has no female relatives of bis own,
will certainly arise and say; “Do not scar his
tender shoulders with the la°h, but lock him up
comfortably, and treat him kindly, and lend him
a tract to read.” For so the world wags, and the
thoughtful ruffian takes note of it. Also when a
worthy wife and mother, who is the mainstay of a
household and the support of a drunken loafer,
gets assaulted by a lamp which bursts like a 10-
inch shell, and wafts everybody but the drunken
loafer aforesaid who bought the kerosene into
glory, the same world sagely remarks: “ Who’d
have thought it? However are people to know
when they are safe?” Which is a ridiculously
insipid conundrum in view of the fact that the
virtues of Luxene, the new illuminant, have been
so freely published and tested that they are now
known to all men—except perhaps the police, who
are so constantly on the track of suburban band ts
and “ fan-tan ” Chinamen that they have no time
to read the papers. But everybody can find out
all they want to know about Luxene by applying
to Messrs. J. Barlow and Co., T. W. Eady and
Son, Mason Bros., Ltd., Tillock and Co., D.
Mitchell and Co., J. Keep and Sons, Holdswortb,
MTherson and Co., M'Lean Bros, and Rigg,
Ltd., J. O. Hanks & Co., Fred. Butcher, and
Robert Miller, Sydney ; J. Ireland, R. Hall and
Sons, and David Miller, Newcastle.

The late Melbourne flood served one good
purpose. It washed all the stuffing out of the
Loyal Orange demonstration at the Exhibition
building. A sense of public neglect incited the
born admirers of the old Dutch Clock to kick up
more dust than usual, but the outside world didn’t
know anything was going on, and the newspapers
took little notice. Who cared about the “ Banks
of Boyne Water ” when the Yarra was brimming
over? Only J. B. Patterson & Co., Limited.

London Figaro, a puny Tory publication
which finds a reader here and there in Australia,
lifts up its little voice to complain of the “atrocious
impudence” of strikers until they are “amply
provided with the sinews of war.” That’s the
point. The dock-labourers for instance, threw up
their income of eight shillings a week and went
rollicking out on “ strike” long before they had a
reserve fund of five millions at the bank. It was
like their atrocious impudence, not to postpone
the matter for another century, or so.

S ; r Lepel Griffin declares that opium-con-
sumption is not synonymous with demoralisation,
that its excessive use is quite unusual, and that its
moderate use in a tropical climate is beneficial.
This quite fits in with the view expressed to The
Bulletin by a surgeon who had practised'3o years
in Hong-kong, and probably knew as much about
the matter as any man on earth. The fact is that
every people has its sedative stimulant. Wine in
one country, tobacco in another, opium in another.
The only people who can do without stimulant
are those who have no nerves—usually because they
have no brains. Abolish rum and you erect a drug-
juggernaut.

The queerest thing in the world was how Fritz Emmet
made his Melbourne hit, with Minnie Palmer’s J. A.
Rogers as his agent. Fritz opened to shy business, and
one night the Governor went, when Emmet chose to get
drunk, and the performance was chopped short, poor
Blanche Stammers running off the stage in a fit of
laughter. After this the larrikins took the show under
their patronage, and it had a tremendous run, with
Emmet’s songs humming unceasingly in the Melbourne
air. Emmet was this kind of man—you would drop in to
see his old familar first act, only intending to stop a few
minutes, but you would be unable to tear yourself away
from his rubbish. R.I.P.

A fellow-passenger of Sara Bernhardt, by the Yankee
mail to Australia, thus wrote from Honolulu .•■—“The
ship had not been out three hours before she had explored
it thoroughly from stem to stern and down into the
hold. One dark night she announced her intention of
mounting to the crow’s nest. It takes a man of good
nerve to do this even in quiet weather, and on this
evening there was a heavy sea running. The captain and
officers urged her not to attempt it, but she laughed at
them and ran up to the foot of the topmast with the
lightness and agility of a cat. The members of her
company, the other passengers, the ship's officers, and
many of the crew had gathered on deck and were watch-
ing her breathlessly as she turned to descend. When
half-way down she stopped, and, clinging to the rigging
with one hand and gesticulating with the other, began
reciting a poem from Victor Hugo. The wind blew her
skirts with tremendous force against the rigging, and the
ship was tossing in a heavy sea, but she held on and went
through the poem.” How truly theatrical!

“Give Wing's to My Soul.”
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
I.
How many old diggers of the early days on North
Gympie recall Mark Foster? He was a great,
long, slouching dyspeptic-looking mixture of the
Victorian and Yankee, who pottered about from
mine to mine, nearly always on his own account,
and always within a couple of days’ rations of the
end of his resources. He looked the embodiment
of melancholy. Those who remember him will,
however, be able to recall many of his sentiments
which were sunny rather than sombre, and a few
of the peculiarities which enlisted the goodwill of
every party of men among whom he fell.
His favourite expression, “ Give wings to my
soul,” was applied to him as a nickname in several
tents, and it is possible that this name outlives the
real one in the memories of many.
He had a peculiar way of drawling those words.
Ordinary diggers’ speech is a forcible article. It
comes from the muzzle as if there were a full
charge of powder at the back and cuts clean to
the ear of the listener. Mark’s method of delivery
was just the contrary. The words themselves
were of the hymnal character and their vocal
vesture matched. “Give wings to my soul.”
Whether it was a snake-yarn of stupendous pro-
perties, a nobbier of extra-fiery draught brandy,
or a lucky discovery among the pebbles in the
creek, Mark’s emotions found an equally satis-
fying vent in “Give wings to my soul.” Where
he picked the expression up was never known. He
was not a reader, nor did he use the words in any
devotional sense. In fact, he was no more
religious than are diggers generally. This was
well-known, otherwise his exclamation would not
have been tolerated, nor would he have been such
an all-round favourite.
It sounds paradoxical, but it is a fact that
melancholy-looking men make the best of boon
companions. Men who seem to have a natural
aversion for society are the pleasantest associates
once they are well in it. So, when Mark dipped his
head and inserted his long body inside the door
of the Mia-Mia Hotel the ruddy-faced diggers
there assembled used to call on him, in one
breath, to push up to the counter. When Mark
complied, as he almost invariably did, the fun
really began, and every time he exclaimed “ Give
wings to my soul,” the spirits of the crowd leaped
several feet higher, and that in the bottles sank
proportionately lower.
But North Gympie began to thin out. Com-
panies with money were laying hold of the likely
spots, and the enterprising free lances had to push
afield. Instinctively they climbed the ridges, but,
when up, some went eastward and some west-
ward, and soon the gay guests of the once
rollicking Mia-Mia Hotel were straying in ones
and twos all over the tableland.
In a western gully Mark pitched his tent. His
liking for a lone hand was undisputed. A mush-
room hotel had sprung up a couple of miles off,
and thither he went at irregular intervals to
exchange a little dust for needful commodities.
Thescene of his work was desolate in the extreme.
A little rivulet dropped languidly from stone tc
stone, curling into pools here and there, but with
a weary gurgle as if it ran to a funeral march
There were trees about, but the bark was hanging
off most of them, like old posters off dead walls
Everybody has heard of the red clay of that
region, but where on Mark’s section the stones
gave place to clay, it was redder than usual. Mark
knocked off three or four pennyweights a day at
the start, but soon even this small assurance of
wealth dwindled down. At last his dish merely
yielded a colour.
It happened at this stage that a flood set in.
The rain poured day and night. The rivulet
became a torrent. Mark moved up the ridge,
exclaiming as he shook his wet clothes and sur-
veyed the scene, “ Give wings to my soul.” Day
after day the rain kept on. It was ten miles to a
store, backwards over the hill. Between him and
the other store a roaring torrent now rolled. His
rations were done. Anyhow he had but a colour
in the match-box in which he carried his dust. He
could, however, obtain credit if the water would
but let him pass.
He could not ford the torrent, and could not in
such weather and over such country tramp ten
miles. He accordingly arranged the fly of his
tent, huddled himself up, wet as he was, in his
damp quarters, and muttered, “ Give wings to
my soul.”
II
The rain ceased, the creeks fell, and men again
moved to and fro. A horseman travelling west-
ward sighted Mark’s tent, and made for it. He
hallooed lustilyallround the structure,(but received
no answer. He coo-eed towards the top of the
ridge and the bottom and to each side, but with
like result. Then he dismounted and thrust his
head into the tent.
A man lay huddled on the ground. A ’possum-
rug and a fevy other articles of clothing were under
and about him, but he and they were saturated
with wet. The man was emaciated to the con-
dition of a spectre, and his first attempts to move
showed that rheumatism had gripped every joint
m his body.
“Hello !” cried the stranger.
Mark's glassy eyes rolled about, and hisblueish-
white lips emitted a groan as he tried to raise one
shoulder.
“So !” said the stranger, going to his saddle and!
returning with a flask. “Brighten up, friend;:
take a pull at this.”
Mark was willing enough to comply, but physical!
disabilities forbade. In a little while, however,
hesueceeded, and soon was able to rise to his feet.
Did you see it ?” were Mark’s first words.
“See what ?”
“ My soul!”
The stranger looked at him a minute. The eyes
were unmistakably the windows of a mad mind.
I lost my soul,” continued Mark, in a low,
even voice, as he hobbled outside the tent. “ Ite
fluttered off. I was lying down there, with a
piece of damper by my side, when it took flight-
A always had misgivings when I said ‘ Give wings
to my soul.’ How did I know I wouldn’t be
taken at my word ? You see, it cannot last. How~
li’o 33,11 a man live without his soul?”
Soul?” echoed the stranger. “What sort of
thing was that?”
“ 1 can’t say I saw it right. It was dusk at the'
time, and the rain had suddenly stopped. So I
urned over, and lifting a corner of the calico, said’
n surprise, ‘ Give wings to my soul !’ Without a
second s warning it rustled off. The wings went
SW i 1 ( ! and it never came back.”
Berhaps,” said the stranger, “if you got on.
and we went over to the hotel, we might'
Couldn’t leave here on any terms. It must-
e somewhere about, and when it sees the state
am in, it will come back. You have your soul
with you?”
The question was abrupt, and the glassy eyes
rolled greedily over the person of the stranger.
“ Yes. I suppose I have ; such as it is.”
“ A fairly good one ?”
“Yes.”
“ Take care of it, friend, take care of it. The
soul is the only portion of you worth thinking of.
It is your proper self. That’s what few under-
stand. If they lost it, like me, they’d find how
mean and paltry is what stays behind. Mere
rags of flesh, dropping away day by day. The
soul lives for ever I I wonder would it be about
the foot of the gully ?”
But Mark was hardly strong enough to go
alone. The stranger, saying that a companion
would arrive shortly, put the patient sitting on a
log, and proceeded to light a fire to make some
tea.
“Come back,” said Mark, plaintively, looking
through the trees at the foot of the gully, “come
back. I feel I am only a brute until you come
back. I’ll not walk upright any more ; I’ll crawl
on hands and feet, like all other brutes.” Then
he glanced furtively at the stranger, and added,
“ He has a soul!”
There was a heavy wedge of wood lying near
the log. Mark’s eyes rested on it a minute, and
again he muttered— “He has a soul. And,” he
continued, “he doesn’t seem to know the use of
it. If mine won’t come back I might buy his.
But then I have no dust. Well ! ” and he looked
at the billet of wood.
The stranger was on his knees trying to kindle
the damp fibres into a fire. Mark moved noise-
lessly from the log, picked up the billet and
approached the stranger from behind.
In a minute there was a “ woosh!” and both men
rolled in the grass, locked in a savage embrace.

IN COLLINS-STREET EAST.
(Our friends from Wagga.)
FIRST: “ Sure there’s nawthin’ but docthors.”
SECOND: “ Yis, there’s a dayl o’ villainy goes on in Milburrn.
HI.
“Of all the bogus science dinned into the
world’s ears medical science, as addressed to men-
tal complaints, is the most colossal fraud. Why,
what do 90 diplomaed men out of 100 know about
psychological derangements? As a mere matter
of fact— can you deny it ? —men get their degrees
in Australian universities without any compulsory
training in psychology at all. Where then is the
cense, when a man’s sanity is under question, in
appealing to such authorities as final judges ? I
have been told it, and on excellent authority, that
men have been hanged in these colonies on medical
testimony as to sanity where the medical men
were known to possess no more special knowledge
■of the subject than the lawyers, the clergymen, or
any other of the merely scholastically-trained per-
sons of the neighbourhood. The fact is that the
average clergyman obtains a better course of
psychological training—a much better—than the
colonial doctor. Yet this latter has actually the
power to send a man to the gallows, sometimes on
this own unsupported testimony, and at other
times with only the corroboration of another like
Mmself. I call this state of things monstrous.”
Dr. Pfeiffer had been nettled by some uncom-
plimentary allusions made to the value of his
foreign distinctions and qualifications. He had
attained an extensive experience in the mad-
houses of Germany and Russia, and on a trip to
Australia had been solicited by the Queensland
■Government to report upon some vexed questions
in connection with the treatment of the insane in
that province. This appointment set the local
medicoes up in arms, and it was to retaliate on
them for their remarks that he wedged into an
after-dinner speech at his club the above denun-
ciation.
On his first asylum visit Dr. Pfeiffer appeared
to be specially struck by the case of Mark Foster.
Mark, he was told, had been an inmate for over
five years and was looked upon now as incurable,
his delusion being that he had lost his soul, and
that, as he could not get it back, he was justified
in taking one when he could. He had killed one
man (for which he was sentenced to be hanged,
for the medical testimony at the trial was to the
effect that Mark was simply a cunning murderer
who concocted the story about his lost soul when
found out), and had attempted the lives of three
others while in confinement.
Dr. Pfeiffer talked with Mark. He listened to
the story, which was told in very much the same
words as when the horseman was listener. He
returned when Mark was in bed. As usual, the
patient was invoking his lost soul to come back.
The doctor joined in the invocation. He went to
the window and opened it. The maniac leaped
from his bed, with hands outstretched, to seize
the doctor by the neck, but the latter was pre-
pared for this and threw him back, without mani-
festing either fear or anger. Then the doctor
talked to him of night and wings in the night; of
lost souls more anxious to find their old bodies
than the bodies were to receive them. He placed
his hands firmly on the shoulders of the maniac,
holding him down, and discoursed rhapsodically,
incoherently perhaps, but with ardour and en-
thusiasm. The talk was all about lost souls,
yearning to return.
He turned to the window. To Mark’s cry,
loud, long, passionate, there came as answer a
swish, a whirr. Wings struck his breast.
IV.
“See here,” said Dr, Pfeiffer, alluding to the
matter afterward ; “no one knows how small a
thing, what a feathery notion, may overbalance
the mind. In some, it is always cn the brink of
the chasm. An instant’s over-excitement or undue
despondency, and it goes. That man, Mark
Foster, was one of these. A bird must have
taken refuge in his tent—possibly the crumbs of
the damper brought it —and on his awakening and
uttering the only poor thing he nursed as a senti-
ment, he look the flight of the frightened bird for
the flight of his soul. The bird I drew in from
the window merely turned his order of thought
back; lifted it back again to the brink of the
chasm. There’s no miracle whatever about it.
He is well now, and may stay so while he lives—
although, of course, no one can be sure.”
The amalgamation of the Exchange Bank
of Australia, Ltd., with the Sydney Deposit Bank,
King-street, is a step in the right direction and as
following in the line of the English banks, a great
number of whom are adopting this satisfactory
method Of concentrating their energies and
capital. The total business of the Exchange
Bank has been merged into .that of the Sydney
Deposit Bank, and under the able management of
Mr. T. S. Richardson, the General Manager, con-
tinued success is 'assured. The latest development
in banking has been promulgated by the Sydney
Deposit Bank who have for some time past given
practical effect to the principle of equitably
dividing banker’s profits with depositors’. The
Exchange Department is very interesting, and
travellers to all parts of the world are informed
that they can obtain or dispose of coins and notes
of all nations, also circular notes of exchange are
issued negotiable in all the principal cities through-
out the world.*
NEW AND OLD.
“ All will be new inside the house ” (Melbourne Royal).
New paint with smell that’s fierce and fresh,
New seats in circle and in stalls ;
New names among the great perfesh,
New decorations round the walls—
All will be new.
New management to run the show,
New shows, each one a “masterpiece";
New treasury with jingling flow,
New enterprise to launch the lease—
All will be new.
Will all be new ? From stage to door ?
With nothing that we’ve known before?
Ah, no ! (and vain is all regret)
Inside the house, it’s safe to bet.
Not all is new.
Old chatter by your gilded cads,
Old jam bestowed for column ads.;
Old plots re-hashed from countless plays,
Old hank’ring after printed praise—
AVill these be new?

A Nhill (Vic.) farmer has raised a cauli-
flower weighing 701 b., measuring 3ft. in diameter*
and 6in. ditto in the stem.
One, Dr. Coruil, stood up in the French
Academy of Medicine the otlur day and unblush-
ingly narrated the result of “experiments of great
scientific interest.” A woman was suffering from
a voluminous breast-tumour. The operator, after
extirpating this tumour, cut off a very small
fragment, and inserted it under the skin of the
other breast, which up to that time was in a
normal condition. This inoculation was made
while the patient was under chloroform with the
most minute antiseptic precautions. Nothing
was observed during the first few days; the skin
was cicatrised, and there was no trace of inflam-
mation. But soon a hard nodule was felt, which
grew, attaining at the end of two months the size
of an almond, and was removed by the same
surgeon.” Dr. Cornil adds, “I received the
fragments of the tumour developed after the
grafting; the tissue of the first and of the second
showed identically the same structure.” He also
recounted another operation of the same kind in
which the poor patient (who was subjected to the
same outrageous cruelty), not wishing to submit
to another operation with the object of removing
the graft, which had become a little tumour, left
the hospital, and had not since been followed up !
“Her Jackeroo.”
He was but a Britisher, it is true, and for many
months succeeding his advent on the Country of
the i achlan his aggravating haw-haw manner and
rather consumptive physique were the scorn of
the loud-voiced, lithe-limbed Australians who
were not afraid of the warrigal, nor timorous in
the pig-hunt. But the little governess at Galah
Station noticed that his voice held music in its
tones, and that his eyes were sympathetic and
dark. And, being of a highly poetic and maternal
nature —rather paradoxical, by the way—she
looked on Henry Grove as her big child, and was
perfectly, though unconsciously, prepared to give
him second place the moment she achieved a child
of her very own. These were the gradations —
first the doll child, then the husband child, and
then the child of the last. He was a colonial-
experience man and had jumped straight from a
clerk’s desk to a jackeroo billet on a sheep-station.
He had been dependent on his clerkship for a
decent living, and ill-health had thrown him out
of that into a mere subsistence. The men of the
station were strong and wild and scornful of
weakness and an incapac’ty and unwillingness
to go out on a periodical drinking-bout. So
Grove knew not them. And his round of duties
made him tired, although he was rapidly securing
sound lungs, and accordingly the super, and the
storekeeper and the other aristocracy of the run
weremot for him either. So he fell back on his
self-appointed mother —the governess, little Ger-
trude Bayley.
The usual result of such a companionship was
forthcoming in this instance. They became
engaged, but Gertrude was Scotchly cautious and
advocated patience.
“Let us wait,’’said she, “until you are quite
well. Then you can go back to an office, and we’ll
get married.” And Grove was forced to be con-
tent with that.”
And so matters remained through the long, wet
winter. August had arrived, and the spreading
stream was beginning to flood and back its waters
for miles over the flats. Every pair of hands was
worth untold gold on the day the water reached
its highest mark, and super. Jackeroo and general
hand were engaged far into the night removing
the stock from positions of danger. The owner
and Ids manager, seeing the water rise as
the night closed in, went down the stream
in the station boat, and brought away all the
women and children but one. They searched for
hours, and then they returned on the back water
and said they thought “the little woman” was
drowned. She had gone to her own room just
before dusk and the water was (> feet deep there
now.
Grove was in agony as he thought on the girl’s
probable fate, and he asked to be allowed to
return to the submerged station.
“ Nonsense, man,” said the manager, “ do you
want to lose your own life too? There’s no hope
for the girl and none for you if you get in there,”
pointing to the racing stream which swept by
them, carrying with it trees, wreckage and the
carcases of drowned sheep and horses.
“If there’s no hope for her, then I don’t want
any for myself,” he replied, and without asking
again for permission, stepped into the boat and
sculled it into the flying water.
It took the boat for a moment broadside on,
threw it like a chip into an eddy and whirled the
little ark on its turbulent bosom.
“Consumptive jackeroo or not,” muttered the
super., “the ringer of us wouldn’t dare more
than
The young man tugged at the oars to warm Ins
chilled blood, for he had been waist-deep in the
water all day. So, aided by his strokes, the boat
rushed through the churning water until, by-and-
bye, he heard a noise as of breakers upon a wind-
swept sea-coast and saw, by the light of the
waning stars, the roofs of the station buildings
groaning and swaying before the weight of the
waters. The flood had spread further, and the
buildings stood in the current.
He fended the boat off from the sides of the
house and guided it through the almost submerged
doorway into what had been the dining-room.
Then, not daring to go further, he raised his voice
above the howl of the hurricane, the rush of the
flood, and the ominous swaying and creaking of
the old homestead, and cried : “ Are you here ?”
Once—twice—no reply, and then a weak voice
said—“ Yes.”
She was within a few feet of him but had been
too weak to speak before. While in her own room,
which was a wooden addition to the main build-
ing, a wall plate had given way and had struck
her, almost stunning her. She had managed to
get on to the dining room table wh ch was of very
heavy and old-fashiom d make, and had heard the
others leave in the boat, but had been too weak to
call. As the flood rose the table had risen with it,
and her only fear had been that she would be
crushed against the ceiling and drowned before
help could come.
As he lifted her tenderly into the boat the
groanings of the doomed house become louder and
the water hissed through the deserted rooms like
a host of serpents. Quickly but steadily he pulled
the boat towards the doorway, passed through it
and headed for the back-water.
The rough oars tore his hands, but ho pressed
on steadily out of the current and had almost
reached the still water when a floating tree struck
the boat and headed her down stream again.
And then there was a sound as of splintering
wood and rending paper, and the homestead, with
one last deep groan and agonised quiver, went
down before the mighty flood.
The boat, despite Grove’s frantic pulling, shot
down the stream like an arrow and passed over
the wrecked building.
And then Grove lost heart and began to joke
feebly.
“ It went first to make a soft bed for us,” he
said.
The boat sped on in the half-darkness and they
had to let her drive without interference until a
new danger threatened them —a great tree-trunk
was racing after their frail craft, and if it struck
them certain death would be the result. In the
face of this peril Grove forgot all fatigue. He
strained at the oars with a perceptibly-increasing
result, but his heart was almost bursting with
exhaustion. A few minutes more and they shot
into smooth water, and the rower, throwing up
his arms, fell backward with the blood oozing
from his pallid lips.
He found himself in a small lessee’s hut next
morning, feeling slightly better for the rough
surgery and make-shift febrifuges administered
him. But then, during the day all his hereditary
gouty deposit evidenced itself, and the wetting of
the previous day brought as its punishment
a neuralgic rheumatism in the hip and knee that
was almost maddening.
And, as is usual in these cases, he became very
irritable with his best friends. Gertrude was well
enough to attend him now, and he watched her
trim little figure brightening that rude hut, to his
eyes, into a palace, and wished that she and the
hut were Ins.
“ I suppose this will put off our marriage again,”
he said grumblingly between the more acute
paroxysms of his pain.
“Well, dear, it certainly keeps us all the
longn- away from the necessary means to start
housekeeping with.”
“You’d better leave me altogether,” he spoke
gaspingly in his agony. “I’m only a drag on
you, and now I’m helpless and only fit for a
coffin.”
But in an instant she was at the side of her big
baby with tender words that quickly dissipated
the gloomy future he had imagined. He wanted
calming in this fashion a dozen times a day.
A few days later she brought him a ticket of
which this is a copy :
Please give
Mr. Grove a bath.
Alf. Lepage.
“You’re to ask no questions, but to go to that
address in Melbourne as soon as you're tit to be
removed.” IShc said, and her wish was law.
They waited a week for an improvement suffi-
cient to warrant the journey, but it did not
appear, and then Grove, longing for re’ief at any
cost, resolved to bear the coach-ride to Hay, the
nearest railway-station, and then to travel round-
about by rail through Albury to Melbourne.
The day after his arrival in the Southern capital
he was carried into a cab and driven to the address
in Lonsdale-street on the ticket given him by his
fiancEe-mother. And over the doors of the large
two-storied building he read the words “The
Bethel Electric Medical Baths Co.” Then he was
carried into the bath, but an hour later could
almost put his feet on the ground as he was helped
out. He had baths every day, and wrote telling
his sensations in his letters to Gertrude.
“As soon as I get the strong currents on,” he
wrote, “ I can almost feel the agony and the old
dead flesh and blood leaving me, and the new li e
of strength coming on. There is no shock per-
ceptible, only a gentle flow of electricity that is
really pleasurable. And the current sensitises the
water, so that you are half floating in liquid waves
of strength—quite different to the waves we
experienced that awful night, I can tell you. That
was the bane and this is the certain antidote.”
“You do not know nor can you imagine,” he
wrote a week later, “ what an utterly new man I
feel. All the nerve-pains are gone, and my
breathing is immensely improved. I intend to
use these Bethel Baths until I am as strong as that
b g man on the old station who used to knock
down horses with his fist. This Bethel— who
sounds Wesleyan but is merely inventive and
commercial —has cured my rheumatics and strung
my nerves again, and I intend that his baths shall
deaden my lung-disease before I come back to
make you Mrs. Groves.”
Well, three weeks later Henry Groves went
back to Galah with a wedding-ring in his pocket
and a strange and almost foreign strength in his
limbs.
This story has to end with a wedding, but its
climax might have been a funeral, and little Miss
Bayley would certainly have died a governess had
not that bath-order happened along in time.

The Story of a Vagrant.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Arthur Jubbins, junior clerk in the Bank of
, Durran, stood on the footpath one
morning about eight years ago, just outside the
bank door. It was not the custom of the bank’s
officers thus to occupy their spare time, though it
was the practice of the clerks in the employ of
Messrs. Dolby and Co., auctioneers, a few doors
higher up, and the young man in the office of
Wakem, the solicitor, a few doors lower down ;
but on this morning, an autumn one, the sun
poured down such deliciously warm rays upon the
footpath, that Arthur Jubbins who was writing
with numbed fingers and chilled blood, felt he
could “ stand it ” no longer, but must go out and
bask in it for a while.
Mr. Jubbins had stood outside his office but a
few moments when his curiosity was excited by
the figure of a swagman some 50 yards distant. It
was not that swagmen were ram avi in the
streets of the pr saio town; but this particular
swagman bore his swag with an apparently
haughty sense of protest—as if it did not belong
to him at all—and, withal, looked so uncommonly
like a “gentleman whohadseen better days” that
Mr. Jubbins turned hastily into the bank and
implored the accountant to watch the swell-mob
swagman go by. To John Field, a new-chum,
swagman were still objects of interest, and his
lively junior never neglected an opportunity of
bringing under his notice anything of “bush”
life that he thought would find a place in Mr.
Field’s next home letter. The next moment the
svvell-swagmen arrived outside the bank window,
and he was observed to halt and study the name
of the bank where it glistened in gold letters on
the wire blind. Then he walked in.
Beyond doubt he was a gentleman. John Field
recognised this fact at a glance. It did not matter
that the stranger’s clothes were dusty and weather-
stained and patohed-up, and with patches that did
not match, and that he bore upon his shoulder a
rolled-up blue blanket, which, with a sense of
relief and proud submission to his hard lot, he
unslung from his shoulder and cast at his feet.
There were two gentlemen in the room—John
Field, the official, dressed in tweeds, and the
swagman, clothed in rags; and the recognition of
personal equality was mutual. Addressing him-
self to Field, the latter said, “Pardon me”—in
a clear, cultivated voice—“have you an officer
in your service by the name of fit. John Bute-
Dalzell ? ”
The enquiry was so different in purpose from
what the accountant had prepared himself to hear,
with his fingers handling some small coin in the
till, that for the moment he was taken back,
looked confused and turned to the junior—Mr.
Jubbins-standing at his elbow, from whom he
enquired—
“ Do you know that name ?”
“ There was a Bute-Dalzell in this Bank a few
years ago, bub I have heard that he left it to go to
Queensland.”
An expression of acute disappointment came
over the face of the stranger as he listened to this
statement of Jubbins. He turned to John Field.
“ This is bad news for me,” he said, “ er—he is
my brother —and I half expected to find him at
this office.”
“Oh, indeed,” John Field remarked, his eyes
speaking an eager sympathy.
‘‘This sort of thing,” and the stranger cast his
eyes over himself as he spoke, “ rather discredits
my statement.”
“ Adversity may overtake all of us, youknow,”
Field responded with delicate interest.
“It does indeed—and I ought not to be in this
position. When my brother, St. John, joined this
bank I was principal of a Melbourne college, but,
however, that’s neither here nor there. I have
just come over from Sydney, where I was chief
leader-writer on the Commercial Thunderblast.”
John Field listened attentively, but without
interrupting, and the ex-guide of Sydney public
opinion, continued;
“ A little friction occurred between myself and
the editor. I was bound to respect my scruples—
and—er—-I suggested, in short, that we were not
in sympathy in our views. In short, I handed in
my pen.
“ I had plenty of money—the Thunderblast
paid well—so I travelled to the Temora rush. I had
bad luck there, lost all, and had to take to the
‘ wallaby ’—as it is called ” —(he pointed sarcasti-
cally to the humble burden at his feet). “I have
managed pretty well to work my way so far. I
am now making for Melbourne, where I have
plenty of friends.” Here he stooped down,
picked up his blue-blanket bundle, and threw it
carelessly over his shoulder, where it sat as
awkwardly as a monkey on the pole of a street-
organ. John Field, who had been much in-
terested in the ragged gentleman’s story,
remarked, kindly—“ Your friends in Melbourne
will help you ? ”
The blue-blanket bundle was dropped on the
floor again, and the owner advanced close to the
counter. “My friends in the metropolis,” ho
said, speaking under a reserve that was plain was
painful to him, “are wealthy and influential,
and have urged me to rejoin them—but pride—
you understand ?”
John Field acquiesced, with a delicate assent
that bespoke a courteous sympathy with the
genteel hardship before him.
“However, 1 am endeavouring now to make
my way down to those friends, and am travelling
by stages to Warradambra, where I have a friend
who will put me right.”
The gentleman in rags paused at this juncture.
Then he went on again, a faint blush pervading
his features : ...
“Painful necessities are sometimes imposed
upon us. The fare by train to Warradambra I
have ascertained to be 7s. fid. It is unfortunate
for me that I have only 45.”
Contrary to the gentleman-in-rags expectations,
John Field, the gentleman-in-tweed did no more
than to listen courteously. He did not volunteer
the expected delicate assistance which rendered
the situation painfully embarrassing to the dis-
tressed speaker, who continued ; “ Would it be
an unpardonable presumption on my part as
brother to a former officer of the bank to ask you
for the loan of —— ? I desire you will under-
stand it to be a loan merely of the few shillings
necessary to carry me on to Warradambra?”
Thus appealed to there was nothing left for the
accountant, having an open till before him, but to
hand to his visitor the sum indicated, which he
did with generous sympathy.
“You will give me your card,” the recipient
observed graciously, his voice trembling with
gratitude ere scraping into his hand from the
counter the “loan,” “and when I reach Mel-
bourne I shall take the earliest opportunity of
refunding this —this loan. I shall never forget
your consideration —never ! I am very much
obliged to you—good morning.” So saying, in a
voice betraying much emotion, the brother, whose
brother was at one time in the bank, withdrew
from the institution, looking even when his back
was turned, a gentleman—aye, every inch.
• » «
Field has now obtained a managership and a
wife, and resides in another part of the country;
and Arthur Jubbins has graduated through the
several stages of promotion, and is himself an
accountant of the bank at an equally important
township, as remote from Durran as the boun-
daries of the colony will permit. Folks who
knew him eight years ago as junior clerk at
Durran no longer recognise him now in his altered
condition of person and fortune. A month ago—
just about eight years since the morning on which
he discovered the “ swell swagman ” coming down
the street of Durran, Jubbins was writing up his
books behind the counter, when the glass door of
his office opened, and a tall, haughty-looking, but
shabbily-dressed individual entered, bearing on
his shoulder a blue-blanket bundle. He looked a
gentleman, despite the raggedness and dust-
stained character of his attire. Yes, above all, he
looked a gentleman, though a gentleman whose
“ better days ” had long gone by.
“ This is the Bank of , I believe,” began
the somewhat haughty, if dilapidated, visitor.
Jubbins assented.
“ Have you an officer in your service by the
name of St. John Bute-Dalzell?”
“We had at one time,” responded Jubbins,
readily, “ but he has quitted the service for some
years. ”
The stranger looked disappointed; then,
brightening up in a moment, he remarked : “Ah,
of course—we have lost the run of each other of
late years—er —he is my brother.”
“ Oh, indeed,” Mr. Jubbins remarked, with
neutral interest.
“That announcement, coupled with my per-
sonal appearance”—and he cast his eyes over him-
self with a well-bred shrug—“will somewhat
surprise you, I have no doubt. But I have met
with misfortunes. I formerly held the position of
principal of College, in Melbourne. I have
just now come over from Sydney, where I was
editor of the Thunderblast. I am in rather an
unfortunate dilemma at the present moment—ef—
I have wealthy and influential friends in the
metropolis, and am anxious to get to Orville to-
night, but I find the fare is seven shillings, and—
really I am ashamed to mention it—when
seeking my ticket at the booking-office I found
I had but three shillings and sixpence about me.”
“ Yes, that is very awkward,” Mr. Jubbins
remarked, coolly.
The distressed visitor continued, with an em-
barrassment of manner pa nful to contemplate—
“ I can assure you the position forced upon me is
very painful to my feelings, and had not my
brother been in this bank I could not bring my-
self to ask of you the loan—only as a loan, you
must understand—of the deficient sum—”
He would have continued, but that Jubbins
broke in with the remark, “It is unfortunate
that you should have happened to call at this
office.”
The other lifted his eyebrows, by way of in-
quiry, but did not speak. He was too well-bred
in manner to attempt.
“Yes,” said Jubbins, as uming the facial
aspect of a judge about to put on the black cap,
ere he proceeded in a calm and deliberate manner
to recall to his mind a visit paid to the bank at
Durran some eight years previously, under
circumstances not dissimilar to the present. He
did not forget to dwell upon his friend
Field’s generosity, which had never been
repaid or acknowledged in any way, nn<j
finally wound up with a scathing denuncia-
tion of the other’s iniquity and impudence.
As soon as he delivered himself of the last word,
however, the miserable culprit remarked calmly—
“ But you won’t refuse me the trifling loan I have
asked, surely ? ”
Jubbins was aghast. “What! ”he exclaimed,
“are you really serious? Why, you must talcs
me for a fool, or a child. To my knowledge now,
you have been travelling for eight years on this
‘racket,’ and yet you—you can stand there and
ask me to help you.”
“Then you refuse to assist? It is a trifling
sum and won’t hurt you.”
“ I do 1 ” shouted Jubbins emphatically.
“Then you are not the man I took you for
and, setting his blue-blanket burden upon his fine,
square shoulders, the dust-stained but gentle-
manly-looking swagman withdrew, gentlemanly
asever.
PELICAN.
A few weeks ago it was announced that
the French, German, and Austrian troops were in
future to be armoured with cuirasses; and now
the further intelligence comes along that every
Swiss soldier is to carry a tin-opener among his
equipment. This looks like business. When the
German enemy comes along in his bullet-proof
case the Swiss warrior will seize him in a calmly
practical way, and open his outer husk with his
tin-opener ; and then he will pick out the occu-
pant, and throw him away down a crevasse. ■

Thus an irreverent contemporary :•*—
The state of California should get a new design for the
marriage licenses vended by county ‘clerks at two,dollars
apiece. The present instrument’ is disfigured with a
picture of an unattractive pair of human beings standing
before a clergyman, in the garb of the Episcopal priest-
hood, with an altar in the foreground. State documents
should have the insignia of the commonwealth upon
them. We therefore move the elimination of the minister
and altar from the marriage license, and the substitution
of a grizzly bear.

In sad, but loving remembrance of my dear husband, Peter
Seres, who was killed at the Lambton Sinking Pit, by
, v the breaking of an old rope, on the 12th October,
1869, leaving me with three children—one three weeks
old—to mourn their sad loss. Also, my beloved hus-
band, Thomas Gillons, who was killed through an old
boiler bursting, at Lambton Middle Pit, June 28,
1890, leaving me one son to mourn his sad loss. Also,
my beloved daughter, Sarah Jane Carlson, who de-
parted this life on October, 14, 1890, leaving five
children to mourn their sad loss.
luserted by their loving wife and mother, {Mrs. S. A,

■ The people of Chicago intend to construct
a new Leaning Tower, after the model of the
-ancient wonder of Pisa. The venerable Italian
castle is 13ft. out of the perpendicular, and the
question that puzzles all antiquarians is—how the
deuce it got to that awful angle and yet didn’t
finish the job by falling over altogether. The new
pork city means to go one better, however, by
sloping its tower 100 ft. off the straight, so as to
make it seven and a half times the more remark-
able of the two. After all, the Yankee is not so
inventive as he looks. He licks creation at copy-
ing the marvels of other lands, and making the
copy 18 times bigger than the original, and as a
fair sample of this weakness he is going to have
the biggest exhibition on record with an Eiffel
Tower several times more gigantic than the
original, and a Leaning Tower in red railway-iron
that leans seven times harder than any such thing
ever leaned before. He wanted also to get the
bones of Columbus and show them round, but as
the Spaniards haughtily declined to dig up their
old hero from his resting-place, he will probably
make a hardwood skeleton of a Columbus 12ft,
high, and show it instead. And then, by the
bye, he will build some fresh pyramids that will
lick Cheops into fits, and put a bogus, dog-skin
Pharoah inside; for the modern American is pas-
sionately fond of antiquities, only he likes to have
them new and fresh and in good condition. He
is a strange individual —a wild symphony in red
paint and pork and pills—full of weird enthu-
siasms and gold-stopped teeth : and for selling a
time-payment organ, or for buying the sacred
scenes of Galilee cheap, and digging them up, and
transplanting them bodily to New York, and then
turning a brass band loose upon them and charging
10 cents admission, he has no equal.
Purves, Q. 0., performed one of his sur-
prising feats as a cross-examiner in the M'Meokan
will-case the other day, when he got a doctor in
the box, and made him faint by reading to him
one of the items out of his own bill. That is to
say, he grasped the bill in his hand, and impaled
the doctor with his eye, and then he read the item
over about 16 times-, till his witness nearly died
through the horrible iteration* There isn’t so
very much in being a great cross-examiner after
all, and Purves’s method is often very simple. He
gets a nervous Witness before him, and looks at
him for a moment •or two with rage and thunder
in his eye. Then he leans towards him, and says
iii a hollow, menacing tone like a dog barking into
an empty jam-tin : “ Is that letter in your hand-
writing?” The witness answers promptly : “It
is.” “Did you write that letter?” The man in
the box looks a little surprised, but replies again :
“ I did.” “Do you mean to tell me ” but
when he has got as far as this the lawyer stops as
if overcome and broken up and shattered by the
astonishing nature of the situation. Then he pulls
himself together, and you can hear the gurgle in
his aorta as he clutches a double handful of papers,
and starts again in a Johnel Sullivan voice : “ DO
you mean to tell me that you— you — you wrote that
letter—DO you say that you would write SUCH
(fearful howl on the “such”) a document as
THAT ? ” (unearthly scream on the “ that ”).
Here the witness staggers and turns pale
and feels that he must have ruined his reputation
somehow over the letter, though there is nothing
in it but a bald statement that he had lost the
9.30 train, and, consequently, couldn’t go some-
where till next morning. But he feebly admits
over again that he wrote it. Then the barrister
turns pale with excitement and suppressed frenzy,
and grabs all the papers on the table, and, with a
shriek that wakes his wife and family in the next
province, he wades in, and the place seems to be
filled with a tangled waste of barks and vocifera-
tions. This time he speaks all in big capitals, and
the smallest words-nearly lift the roof. “DO
YOU ACTUALLY STATE YOU WROTE
THAT LETTER ?” The testifier feels that the
Last Day has come, and collapsing in the box,
says, “I did.” The lawyer glares horribly in the
empty air : wildly casts his papers at the roof;
and for want of anything else'to seize he grabs
the table itself, and with it in his arms fires his
last howl. “ You DID ! ! !!” There is no answer,
for the witness is in an insensible condition on
the floor. That is the way to: be a Great Cross-
examiner. c

Dead, but not forgotten.
My friends, I feel it sad to think
My lot has been so hard
To see two able-bodied men
Cut off without a word.
And I am left to do my best;
No one seems to notice,
Although the thing so terrible,
They seem to take no notice.
The dead, they feel no pain ;
, The living have to suffer,
Although the ropes and boilers
Were not worth a snuffer.

The number of divorces annually granted
in the UtS. is greater than the sum of the divorces
and separations in all. the rest of the Christian

At the conclusion of the Wiltshire murder
trial, at Adelaide, at which trooper Willshiro
was honourably acquitted, his boss, Police Com-
missioner Peterswald, ostentatiously shook hands
with him.
Dear Bulletin, — It was Detective “Dick”
Elliott, now retired, and who may be seen any day
on the King-street (Sydney) block— not Mr.
Inspector Oamphin, who “dropped” to Nichols’
and Lester’s little game in the advertising line.
Elliott had had Nichols in his care some years
previously for forgery or something of that sort,
and recognised his handwriting the moment he
saw it. Oamphin arrested the miscreant Lester,
alias Froude. The latter was said to be highly
connected.—F.
A wandering revivalist has been warming
up a Maoriland town lately. Hand bills were
scattered broadcast h aded, “A voice from tho
Condemned Cell,” and setting forth that the Rev.
John Mac Neill, 8.A., will “deliver a message
from a young man executed in Sydney in connec-
tion with the Mount Rennie outrage,” and invites
all, “especially young men and young women.”
The rev. gentleman, according to an M.L. paper,
has had packed houses—especially of women. He
takes up a collection at the end to defray
expenses. Rev. Butcher Yarley might here learn
a wrinkle er two.
Lord Normauby : “I believe that women
have no appreciation of simplicity in dress, nor in
anything else. I believe that the more a man
bedizens himself with velvet, satin, gold chains,
rings on his firrgers, and varnished boots, tho more
they admire him. For example, for seven years I
carried a cane which I felt was a degradation to
me. It was a brown cane; the upper part
was made entirely of turquoises ; it was a most
disreputable cane. It Was given to me. So long
as I carried that cane I was ail powerful. Every
woman succumbed the moment she saw that
cane; they felt that therein was wealth, splendor,
etc. I lost it. From that hour my power ceased,
and I never regained it.”
The Maorilanders are thinking of sending
P. A. Buckley, of the Upper House, home to
London to be their new Agent-General. Buckley
is a loud and warlike Irishman, and the Upper
House would be glad to get rid of him, for he is
not solemn enough for the billet. He has no
extraordinary intelligence, but still lie has about
enough to crowd through, and he has two great
qualifications for the ambassador's job. First, he
can speak quite a lot of languages, most of them
pretty badly, so that if any foreign power wanted
to take his country in he could go out and talk to
it, and as neither party would be likely to know
what the other was saying no bad results could
very well ensue. Secondly, he is not likely to
ooinmit hMs country to any warlike experiments,
or to encourage any Soudan contingents or
similar foolishness, for he took a hand in a con-
tingent himself when young, and there was so
little glory and so much hunger and mud and
rheumatism, that he is most unlikely to want any
more of the kind. Buckley went out along with a
good many other Irishmen to fight for the Holy
Father, against Garibaldi’s rod-shirts, and very-
few @f that Irish Brigade over came back- They
got no pay, very little provisions, and a good deal
of the Holy Father’s benediction. Some of them
were killed in battle; and a lot more were
wounded, and then some amateur surgeon tried to
amputate their damaged limbs with an axe and a
shovel, and finished them off. Some of them were
left crippled on the field, ere a kindly Garibaldian
came along and finished them with a fence-rail,
for the Liberator’s army consisted of a lot of nob e
patriots in front and a lot of irredeemable ruffians
behind. Some of them were captured and hung
surreptitiously ; some disappeared mysteriously 0
others eloped with strange Italian females whose
husbands followed them and shot them in the
night; and a few got into trouble through serenad-
ing girls whs didn’t belong to them. And taking
them all round they had a very bad time, and
Buckley has never gone fighting for any other
Holy Father from that day till this.
The great official intellect shines out very
conspicuously whenever it gets a fair chance.
When some poor wretch has been run in, and his
clothes embezzled, and his boots collared, and his
hat boned, for the same reason, the police
straightway communicate a paragraph to the
evening papers setting forth that they want an
owner for one blue tweed overcoat, one pair gray
pants slightly frayed at the feet, serge jacket, and
waistcoat, and a silver Waltham watch, &c.
Then in about half an hour the air is full of men
all in want of an outfit, and all bounding madly to
the police-office to claim the property. And
the description furnished by every one of
them agrees so exactly with the descrip-
tion m the newspaper that there isn’t the
slightest doubt that the whole lot of them is the
only real owner. The world is choke-full of
owners—it is the property that isn’t sufficiently
copious to go round. Meanwhile the man who
was,arrested is under lock and key so that he
can’t bound in with the rest and lodge a des-
cription and claim the goods, sonsequently, who-
ever gets the things, he is left every time. And it
never seems to strike anybody that the system
too brilliant and too successful
for if the police arrested the Union Bank, and
then announced that an owner was wanted for a
eapjtd of £1,000,000 and a reserve fund of
£BOO,OOO, both of which were awaiting identifica-
tion, somebody would burst round the corner a
minute later, and prove that he had lost these
u 4 s , a hancJ y wa y of getting a winter
outfit the best dodge we know is claiming stolen
property, and the next best dodge is identifying
lost luggage at the railway station. N.B. To
work this last dodge inquire either for a well-worn
travelling bag with a number of defaced labels on
ior else for a tin trunk. There are always about

The latest London dodge foT getting cheap
liquor is to order your favourite tipple, empty a
white powder into it, drink four-fifths of it at one
gulp, and then commence bidding earth farewell.
At this stage the_ publican, fearing you’ll die of
poison on the premises, and bring his extra-mild
into disrepute, fires you out on the way to the
next pub., forgetful of your indebtedness.
The proposed penalty for making a false
declaration within the provisions of the Land Bill
now before the iS'.Z. Parliament is imprisonment
for “not exceeding five years” and forfeiture cf
lease or license. For proposing to accept any
bribe to abstain from competing for lands (black-
mail, in short) a term not exceeding 12 months, or
a fine up to £2OO ; for actually accepting bribe, two
years and £5OO.
Illustrated London News (which is
understood to be the Liberal paper of its class —
the Graphic is Tory), in describing Miss Albeita
Victoria Ponsonby’s wedding, pours out the
following sickening slush at the loot of the altar
and throne :
The lucky man to whom this prize has fallen is Col.
Montgomery, of Grey Abbey, County Down. Col. Mont-
gomery served with distinction in the Zulu War of 1879,
but military renown fades before the achievement of
marrying a god daughter of the Queen, and in the pre-
sence of snch an array of Royalty as seldom graces the
domestic festivities of subjects.
Bwoogh ! Military renown is in itself a ghastly
glitter that lowers the human race, especially when
the hireling butcher, at the nod of a king,
goes forth to kill half-armed niggers at so much a
day ; but this sort of greasy grovel suggests quag-
mires alive with slimy, wriggling worms and
squash-toads, and limbless, foul-smelling, squirm-
ing reptiles. The English illustrated press is the
stipendiary agent of Loyalty, which is another
name for the apotheosis of vulgarity. It deifies
insignificance, ennobles sordid wealth, shores tp
the rotten edifice of hereditary titledom, and pre-
sents to the human eye as refined and artistic all
that is really coarse and revolting in the realms
o c caste and privilege. The heavy-jowled, heavy-
feeding Guelphs are, in its pages, beautiful for
ever. The coarse, sensual Royal glutton is tin
Apollo—the vacuous stare of hopeless imbecility
is carefully extracted from the long face of his
nibs with the sheep-hurdle collar. The British
illustrated paper is nothing but a Royal posting-'
bill, and we never look at its Royal processions
unless the doctor has ordered us an emetic.

Two Men Wrong—One Man Right.
“ You will be in kingdom-come in less than twelve
months! ”
“ You can't live three years I ”
“ You are not going to die! ”
These three prophecies were addressed to the
same man. The last one had the most comfort in
it, yet he couldn’t tell which had the most inspira-
tion.
Here’s the story. It isn’t pleasant reading at
first, but it “ comes out ” all right, as the children
say. _ .
Perhaps it’s better to let our friend tell it him-
self. We all like the pronoun “I,” that’s why
an autobiography is never dull. This relator is a
butcher of Auckland, N. Z., and his style is so
clear and good, it needs no editing.
He says ; “ Some five years ago, when lifting a
quarter of beef, I noticed a remarkable soreness in
the small of my back. I called a doctor, gave up
business, and took to my bed. He prescribed a
lotion to be rubbed in around the region of the
kidneys. This was done, and I remained in bed
several days, suffering excrutiating agony. At
last, haying examined the kidney secretions, the
doctor said, ‘You have Bright’s disease, and will
be in kingdom-come in less than twelve months,’
“Never believing I had Bright’s disease, I
thought, however, it would be as well to insure
my life. On examination, the company’s physician
refused to pass me for life insurance, saying,
‘ You can’t live three years.’
“About three months afterwards I was again
seized with a severe attack, and went about more
dead than alive. Finally I broke down and again
took to my bed, as was supposed, for the last
time, I sold one of my businesses so as not to
leave too many affairs for my wife to sunervise,
made my will, and explained to my wife what to
do in case of my death. I can well remember her
listening with tears in her eyes to what she be-
lieved were my last instructions. I then lingered
for some weeks, waiting for death to free me from
my sufferings-taking whatever medicines were
prescribed, but deriving little or no relief.
“One evening a friend called, and we talked
over my case and the very serious turn it had
taken. Presently he said, ‘You are not going to
die. Have you tried Seigel’s Syrup yet ? ’
“ I confessed that I had not. In fact, I had
religiously followed the instructions of my medical
advisers. My friend persuaded me to try Seigel’s
Syrup, and I began at on»e, taking twenty-drop
doses according to the directions. In about a
week I felt a little more life in me, and after
having finished the second bottle I was convinced
that improvement had fairly set in. I continued
taking the Syrup until I had consumed ten bottles,
then went downstairs to work again, still using
the Syrup. After having taken from eighteen to
twenty bottles altogether, I found myself entirely
well. It is now four years since I took my last
dose of Mother Seigel’s Syrup for Bright’s disease
of the kidneys, and I have suffered no symptoms
of kidney complaint since.
“ (Signed) Robert Hutchinson, Butcher.
Wellington-street, Auckland, New Zealand.”
We print Mr. Hutchinson’s statement as he
wrote it, yet it is not probable that his malady
was actually Bright’s disease, as that is a degenera-
tion or destruction of the substance of the kidneys,
and difficult, if not impossible, to cure. What he
really suffered from was, no doubt, a debilitated
state of the system, originating in indigestion and
dyspepsia, with severe kidney symptoms, as is often
the ease. When we remember that nearly all
maladies, including rheumatism, gout, consump-
tion, liver complaint, heart disease, &c., are due
to poisons in the blocd, arising from fermented
food in the digestive tract, we can see why Mother
Seigel’s Syrup is victorious over so great a variety
of apparently diverse diseases. And because of
the one source of these complaints, serious and
often fatal mistakes are made in the diagnosis and
in the treatment. The point to emphasise is this :
Heart disease, consumption, and Bright’s disease
are much rarer than they are supposed to be.
Generally what seems like them is a group of
symptoms of indigestion and dyspepsia. It is
always best to take the advice of Mr, Hutchinson’s
friend when he said, “You are not going to die ;
try Seigel's Syrup.”
Men Who Rose from the Ranks.
This is the heading of a paragraph in a Sydney
weekly, conducted by a family of eminently pious
professing Christians. Amongst others they
mention that “ Brindley, the engineer, Captain
Cook, the famous navigator, and Robert Burns,
the poet, were originally ordinary day labourers.”
That's all perhaps they were.
Then they give us some masons and bricklayers.
I’ll come to them last.
But take this ;
The carpenter's shop has given us Inigo Jones, the
architect; Harrison, the famous chronometer-maker; John
Hunter, the eminent physiologist; Romney and Opie, the
painters ; Professor Lee, the great Oriental scholar ; and
John Gibson, the sculptor.
Are these the biggest swells you can mention ?
Don’t you think, in common decency, you ought
to have put in the Carpenter who, to this day, has
such a crowd of followers? Shame on you ! That
is, if you believe what you pretend.
Then you name Arkwright, Tenterden, and
Turner as barbers. Not a word about the woman
who stopped the Syrian Jack the Ripper by shav-
ing his head.
Lindsay, the ship-owner, was a cabin-boy. So
was the man who lived under a pumpkin-vine and
afterwards in the innards of a cretacean ; at least,
he was entered on the books at a shilling a month.
But your masons and bricklayers do get
me. You can’t have forgotten the poor widow’s
son whose name is at the bottom of this para-
graph. He is said to have taken a big hand in the
building of a finer tabernacle thananyPresbyterian
show you may sleep in. But the contemptuous
way in which you mention the different trades !
Don’t, brother or brethren Smoothfacts, sneer at
labourers in contradistinction to the condition to
which they have risen (?) Of the fifteen or twenty
you mention two at least were drunkards, two
others were accused of peculation. I remember
well when a fifth one of them shot himself, and, if
you wish to give your female relatives a treat, I’ll
mark a page or two of the writings of another for
a Sunday afternoon’s quiet reading in their
boudoirs.
I now caH to your mind another mason or
bricklayer. Perhaps you know more about him
than I do. I refer to Him who built the High
Heavenly Temple.
HIRAM ABIFF.
P.S.—I beg to remind you also of the shepherd-
boy with his shanghai, and one Peter a whaler.

“Hard Times Come Again No More.”
Such is the mocking song of the Melbourne
G.P.O. chime bells, at three o’clock. Such things
raise the bile, and are a tocsin of revolution, just
opposite De Freitas’s eight o’clock rush of the
hungry unemployed for a free meal.

Co operative Irrigation.
An unemployed painter and house-
decorator applied verbally for the vacant position
of Melbourne Town Clerk just as the nominations
were closing the other day.. They told him he
“looked more like stone-breaking,” and, indeed,
he deserved the compliment. Precedent decrees
that only a pompous toady can adequately fill the
vacant stool.
The operation of the Queensland act which
empowers local bodies to rate on the unimproved
land values is making the Brisbane capitalist sit
up. One big city firm had their rateable
value raised to £61,000 and rose up to object there-
to; but as they’ve a £40,000 building on the land
and an £BO,OOO mortgage over the lot—a mortgage
being generally estimated at one third of the
security—they sat down and paid up or will pay
up when the time comes unless they’ve sold out to
somebody else." Which reminds us that financing
is a varied and peculiar thing, for one of the partners
valued the property on behalf of the company
making the advance and the other as a director
thereof solemnly assisted in authorising same.
Wherefore we fancy the Municipal Council might
as well have stuck on another £lOO,OOO and then
sent round its collector in the sure and certain
hope of gathering in the greed.

The Maybrick trustees will again sue the
insurance-company in November for the amount
of the policy effected on the life of James May-
brick, poisoned by his wife, now suffering im-
prisonment for life. Thus, by a side-wind, will

evidence concerning the cause of James May-
brick’s death.
Gamblers are not, as the Australian clergy
appear to think, actuated by mere love of money,
but, like Don Juan, by love of love— i.e., their
gamble.
A Melbourne barber, in Melbourne, re-
cently filed his schedule and set down his assets as
a razor and pole. Which reminds us that, under
the old law, Judge Barry (Vic.) had a petition for
sequestration put before him, in forma pauperis,
wherein the assets were set down as nil! “ What’s
this, sir?” said the Judge, in his pompous way.
“ I wish to sequestrate my estate, your Honor ! ’
humbly responded the bankrupt. “Estate !
Estate! You have none, your assets are nil! Go
and buy a razor and set your assets down at one
shilling, and bring your petition back to me.”
The poor bankrupt did as directed, surrendered
the razor to the assignee, and was whitewashed
accordingly.

“THE BULLETIN.”
(Published at Sydney, N.S.W.)
The Uniqnc Weekly—Australia's Nonpareil
Illustrated Newspaper.
“Tlxo Bulletin,”
Premier Australian Literary Journal,
Is the only paper which has a consistent and dis
tinctive Australian National Policy,
The Bulletin favours—
A Republican Form of Government.
Payment of Members.
One Person, one Vote,
State Revenue derived directly from the Land.
Complete Secularisation of State Education.
Reform of the Criminal Code and Prison
System.
A United Australia and Protection against
the World.
The Bulletin denounces—
Religious Interference in Politics.
Foreign Titles.
The Chinese.
Imperial Federation.
The public eye rejects as uninteresting more
than half of what appears in the newspapers of
the day. It is only the remainder that is found
in The Bulletin.
The Bulletin is an aggressive Democratic
paper which strives to exclude from its artisti-
cally-condensed columns all matter which is not
of general human interest. The Bulletin was
started eleven years ago with no capital but brains,
and has become a vast property, because it pos-
sesses a vitality lacked by the countless news-
paper-ventures in which Australian capitalists
have unavailingly spent large fortunes.
The Bulletin’s conductors claim that every
progressive paper on the Australian continent has
more or less endeavoured to profit by its example
and follow its lines. The result of its powerful
influence and its unprecedented literary and com-
mercial success can at once be seen by comparing
the methods pursued by the Australian daily and
weekly press prior to and immediately after the
first issue of The Bulletin.
From the date of The Bulletin’s inaugural
publication its success was assured. It had at
once a dozen more or less servile imitators, many
of which even took, so far as was possible, its
name. They are now all dead, while The Bul-
letin continues the one phenomenal success in
Australian journalism, the one completely non-
local and distinctly universal Australasian news-
paper.
The Bulletin proprietary have spent some
thousands of pounds in fitting their buildings
with the latest appliances for high-class printing
and the production of illustrations, and they have
now issued The Bulletin in an entirely new
form—printed with new type of improved legi-
bility on the finest paper, and adorned with
sketches from the hands of the ablest and best-
paid newspaper artists Australia has ever seen.
The Bulletin is the only well-established Aus-
tralian journal which, throughout its career, has
been consistently Radical. It points with pride
to the fact that for years it stood alone among
secular newspapers in advocating the Irish
National Cause, and that it was the only Aus-
tralian journal which denounced the Soudan
Expedition from its inception.
The Bulletin is the beacon-fire of national
progress ; it is the forceful summons to the
crusade against the monopolies in land, wealth,
power, and privilege —the direct cause of half the
misery which now afflicts British humanity.
Against the claims of avaricious clergy who claim
to monopolise salvation, against the dark despotism
of grasping plutocrats, and against the exercise of
any human authority which has not been granted
by the people over whom it is exercised, IHE
Bulletin will fearlessly apd ruthlessly contend.
The Bulletin, unlike the ordinary weekly
journal, is not loaded with a weight of matter
transferred from the columns of a daily paper
issued from the same office. The Bulletin is
not connected with any other paper, and the
matter it contains is all specially prepared by its
own unequalled staff.
The Bulletin, which is the only paper sold
by runners in the streets of every Australasian
capital, is the most widely-circulated of all papers
published south of the equator, and is the only
Australasian publication which enjoys a popu-
larity unhampered by the political boundaries
that so closely restrict other journals to tne
colonies in which they are printed.
The Bulletin’s red cover is equally familiar
to the bushman of the Far North, the stockman
of Central Australia, the pearl-sheller of ion'te
Straits, and the digger in the New Zealand
Ranges. A paper which is at once the most
popular city publication and the organ ot tne
intelligent bushman must indeed be broadly based
Perhaps the strongest proof of The Bulletins
popularity is found in the unchallengeable fact
easily ascertainable from any postal official-Imu,
of all journals issued in Australasia, iHE dui.
letin is the one most frequently remaned iron
one reader to another.
The Bulletin is the journal which, of all Aus
tralian journals, is most frequently quoted in i K
land, America, and the colonies.
The Bulletin is going ahead faster than any
other paper, because its vast mass of original an
specially-written reading matter is the brign
and best, its illustrations the cleverest and i
artistic, and its policy the boldest ami m
straightforward ever submitted to the Austra
public.

_ „ 10th December, 1888.
To H. F. KbOELMANN, Esq.
Dear Sir,—This is to certify that I, Patrick Keenan, w
taken ill about three years ago with a severe cold, whii
turned into Bronchitis and became chronic. 1 consult
more than one doctor, and during that time took a gre
quantity of medicine without it doing me the slighte
good, but 1 became worse every day, wasting away urn
1 became a perfect skeleton. About February last I bogs
to spit up blood, and what with the cough and seve
pains m my left lung, and the quantity of bad mattei
was expectorating every day, I became very much alarme
Hearing of your professional skill I consulted you, ar
you told me you could cure me for a given sura. I agree*
and went under your treatment in March last. At tl
end of three months I was taken bad one morning, ar
vomited about two pints of blood, matter and strim
phlegm. From that time I commenced to improve, ar
was gaining in weight every day. About a month ago tl
blood ceased coming, the cough disappeared, and tl
lung healed completely, and thank God and your pr
fessional skill, I am as well as ever.
P. KEENAN.
Declared and subscribed before me, one of Her Majesty
Justices of the Peace, for the Southern Bailiwick, in tl
County of Grant, this 10th day of December, 1888.
Peter Smyth®, J.P,
